CAPE TOWN, March 6. â Nearly the whole of the Dutch popu
lation of the Prieska and Kenhardt districts are in rebellion. Many
of the Dutch from neighboring districts are reported to have joined
them, notably Piet Moolman, who will lead the rebels to Brandbles.
Lucas Steinkamp, commanding the Boers, is reported marching on
Carnarvon. It is believed that Gordonia and Victoria West will
rise. It is thought that the total number of men in arms will
exceed 3000. The general opinion of loyalists is that a strong
force of British will be required as a reverse would spread the
rebellion.

Desires to Prevent an Outbreak of
Hostilities Until the Completion of
â¢ the Trans-Siberian Railway.
Special Cable to The Call and New York Herald. Copyrighted, 1900, by
the New York Herald Company.
YOKOHAMA, March 6.â No one of
the legation In Toklo nor any of the
Japanese officials will acknowledge
that war with Russia is imminent,
and it Is well, known in diplomatic
circles that at present Japan and Russia
are working secretly to obtain concessions
in Korea against other nations; but this
does not mean that a clash between Rus
sia and Japan Is not coming In the future.
Japan is the only serious obstacle In the
way of Russian Eastern policy. Japan
must have Korea, which already is flood
ed with Japanese coolies, who. are really
soldiers. /
Â¦Russian transports pass through Naga
saki constantly laden with troops for Port
Arthur. The Japanese common people
have a great hatred for Russia and
would welcome war without counting the
cost: but they have little power. Japan
has not money for war and Russia will
vleld to Japan so long as the trans-Si
berian railroad is Incomplete. An Ameri
can engineer. who has been over the road
reports that it cannot be used effectively
for nine months.
* Rumors of an impending war originated
In Shanghai, - not In Japan, and were
based on generalizations, not on facts.
Russia Is now changing her representa
tives throughout Japan. %nd would not do
so if she expected an immediate outbreak.
As during the last three years a crisis
resulting in war might arise at any mo
ment; but for the present It Is not at
hand, and in my opinion Russia can pre
vent it until she Is ready.
RUSSIA NOT YET READY
FOR WAR WITH JAPAN
COLONIALS CONTINUE
TO DEFEAT BOERS
DORDRECHT. March 5.â Following yes
terday's success. General Brabant again
e-ctured the Boers to-day with advan
tage, holding the position captured ye.
terday There was some smart fighting
this morning, the British losing five or
six men and capturing the Boer fort, thus
vastly improving their position. The
Boers fought tenacious^'.. contesting every
inch of the ground, but ultimately they
retreated suddenly, carrying off their guns
aid wagons. A mounted force pursued
them but the result is not yet known.
The British casualties during the two
tfays were thirty wounded and twelve or
thirteen killed. The Boer losses are un
known. Throughout the arduous fight and
eevere fatigue the Colonials have behaved
splendidly. ,

MONTEREY MAN KILLED.
MONTEREY. March 6.â News comes
here that a former Montereyan, John
Todd, has been killed while fighting in
South Africa with the Royal Canadian
Infantry against the Boers. The details
have not yet been learned here and It is
only known that he was killed during
Lord Roberts' campaign against General
Cronje.
Todd enlisted In the United States army
at the outbreak of the war with Spain
and served to the end of his term in the
Philippines. Afterward he joined- the
Royal Canadian Infantry for the South
African war and has been with that regi
ment through all Its hard fighting. .

party In less than an hour, bat they met
greater difficulties after that time. It was
then that telegrams were sent to Mont
gomery. Charleston and other places for
physicians . nurses and caskets, but dur
ing the greater part of the day there was
use only for the caskets.
All work was stopped In the surround
ing mines of the district and the people
within a radius of many miles assembled
on the grounds of the Red Ash Company.
As the victims Were brought to the sur
face and carried away on the hastily con
structed stretchers it was frequently no
ticed that many met their husbands,
fathers, brothers and sons among the res
cuers when they thought these men were
In the mines. The meetings between the
men who had not yet entered the mines
and their families were very touching. AH
those who could not find their friends In
the crowd assumed that they were In the
Inclosed mines.
Grief of the Relatives.
The population of the mining village Is
only 1500. AH are miners and very many
of them are related and they all know
one another. There was great distress all
day among those who could not find their
friends or hear from them. As Tliunnond
Is only three miles from the scene of the
disaster there were some few in the mine,
who lived at that place, but for the most
part the victims resided here, and the
calamity will reach almost every little
house in the mining town. None of the
people here have been at their homes dur
ing the day, but all have remained around
the Red Ash mine. Some have not eaten
during the day. Food was liberally sup-,
plied to the relays of workmen in the res-,
cuing party, but many of the women who
could get no word of comfort refused to
eat or go to their homes.
At a late hour to-night the most relia
ble estimate obtainable put the number
of victims at fifty. The capacity of tho
mine is 173. but there have "been only 130
on the payroll so -far this month, and it
is said that less than half of these were In
Â¦ the mine at the time of the explosion.
The manager, superintendent, bosses,
bookkeepers and others aro very busy
with the force of .rescuers, but at the
same time they are all very reticent a a
to. the extent of the . casualties and any
other information regarding the disaster.
Awful Sights Witnessed.
The scenes around the homes where tho
bodies are lying to-night have drawn some
of the Immense crowd away from the
workmen at the entrance of the mine.
Those working on the rescue relays say
that the scene becomes more terrible as
they get farther Into the mine.
Bodies that cannot be Identified or
recognized have been placed In tho
large . blacksmith shop of the Red
Ash Coal Company, and that place
presents the appearance of a horrible
morgue. Although the bodies are man
gled beyond recognition, they are sur
rounded by those who are in distress and
hunting their lost friends.
The general belief is that the explosion
occurred from contact with dust when
the miners entered, this morning with
their lights, and that it was not due to
fire damp, as had been currently reported
all day. Nearly all the men employed In
the mine were white, only about one-fifth
being colored, and most of the men were
married, and had families in the little cot
tages near the tipple of the great mine.
A special train arrived from Montgom
ery at 7 o'clock to-night, filled with re
porters, physicians, nurses and others,
and one car contained thirty caskets.
After the arrival of the train, an order
was sent back to Montgomery for fifty
more caskets. Another train arrived from
Charleston with physicians, caskets and
relief supplies. Still another train came
In frorn-'Hlnton. but there Is no need of
physicians and nurses, as evidently all of
the vicUins are dead. The work of rescue
is being continued during the night and
will be kept up until the mine is clear.
FIRE CREEK. W.. Va.. March 6.â
The most disastrous mine explosion
ever known in the New River dis
trict occurred at the Red Ash
'mine shortly after the miners went
to work early this morning. Although
the most heroic work of the rescuing
party has been going on incessantly all
day, it Is Impossible to-night to estimate
the full extent of the loss of life and prop
erty. . . -
Ten bodies have already been taken out,
and the total number of victims will
reach fifty. There Is little hope for those
still In the mine, and who number at least
forty. The cause of the explosion can
only.be conjectured, but it is believed to
have resulted from dust, as was. the case
in a Pocahontas mine some years ago.
The Re.d Ash mine is a large drift, and
the explosion occurred near the entrance,
which was thus closed by the falling
slate, entombing a large number of
miners. The scene of the disaster is be
tween this place and Thurmond, on the
south branch of the Chesapeake and
Ohio Railroad. J. Fred Efflnger of Stan
ton, W. Va.. is the principal owner of the
mine.
Relief parties from great distances ar
rived as soon as possible. State Mine In
spector Plnckney, with a corps of ex
perts, has been on the ground during the
day rendering all assistance possible and
devoting his attention more toward re
lief than to an official investigation as
to the cause of the disaster.
Rescue Work Impeded.
The managers and bosses of all the
mines in this district came to the scene
and joined in the work of rescue, but they
were greatly impeded by the extent of
the enormous blockade at the entrance to
the drift. The large . side tiers of the
entrance were blown out, together with a
lot of heavy timbers. Even -mules were
hurled some distance. The force of such
an explosion caused an immense falling
of slate and other debris, so that the en
trance was filled. As many men as could
work at one time were digging away with
all their might, and these were relieved in
short relays ; by, other men, so as to ex
pedite the . work as much as possible.
t The first successful strike of the rescu
ers was at 10 a. m... when ten men were
recovered. Seven of them were already
dead and the other three were dying.
As the miners had fallen at different
places in the drift, and the explosion had
caused the falling slate to blockade the
rooms In different parts of the mine, the
work of rescue met one. great obstruction
after another. The men reached at 10
a. m. were not far from the entrance,
while others were at much greater dis
tances within the drift.
At the mouth of the mine the scene
was heart-rending. The wives and chil
dren and neighbors of those who were
known to be entombed were there in full
force and their distress was pitiable.,
While they were all seeking to help those
who were rescued and to get the mine re
opened, yet these bereaved people were
for the most part in the way of the res
cuers and had to be held back from the
entrance.
Fail to Pump in Air.
The mine is one of the largest in West
Virginia and was very heavily timbered
In the different drifts. It was for this
reason at once feared that those who had
not been killed by falling debris at the
time of the explosion were pinioned by
these connected timbers and might suf
fer death from' suffocation. Air was
forced into the mine by engines on the
surface, which were kept working after
the explosion, but it was found that air
could be pumped into the drift for only a
short distance, .as the coal, stone and
earth shut off all avenues. The pumps
and all other machinery in the mine were
demolished so that everything had to be
done through temporary arrangements on
the surface.
After the men got under headway In
the work of rescue they reached the first

After Two Years of a Deep Sleep, From Which the Efforts
of a Score of Physicians Gould Not Wake Her,
She Passed Quietly Away. V
MISS ELIDA WlfcBUR, WHOSEISIiEEP ENDED;IVITH HER LIFE.
mISS ELIDA WILBUR, who " for
two years lay unconscious to all
her surroundings, baffling the
constant attention and efforts of
skilled physicians, passed to the great be
yond yesterday morning. She died at her
mother's home, 2228 California street,
never having regained consciousness since
the fateful day when she was found part
ly asphyxiated by gas.
Full of sad and pathetic incidents are
the last few years of Miss Wilbur's life.
A young girl In the bloom of life, beauti
ful of face and figure, courted by her
many friends and one of the most popular
young women in society, she was cut off
from the world by a single and fearful
stroke of blighting misfortune. When the
which marred her life and re
sulted ultimately in her death occurred,
she was the betrothed of James Dunphy.
the only son of one of California's
wealthiest cattle men. Â¦ From prospects of
a glowing, and happy future she was sud
denly launched into a world of darkness
and all the blessings of nature and the
happiness of her social life gave place to
a misery as complete and perfect as is
visited upon mortal being. y
A Living Death.
The 'accident which made Miss Wilbur
the "living dead" for over two years hap
pened on the evening of February 16, 1803.
She and her betrothed James Dunphy. had
been visiting a mutual frtend. Returning
to the young lady's home at an early
hour, Mr. Duriphy did > not leave for some
little time after. "It was rumored- at" the
time that the lovers had quarreled, and
that he left the house unreconciled and de
termined to break the engagement exist
ing between them. The next morning Miss
Wilbur was found sitting In a large, easy
chair -In her room. A< gas Jet was open
and she was in 'a 'state of complete un
consciousness. : "Â¦Â¦' Â¦- : ' ':. ' : .
The circumstances and conditions of ithe
case scouted the idea or theory rof at
tempted suicide. .'A window 'in the room
was wide, open and. Miss Wilbur's posi
tion, recumbent, and, a book . which s she
had been reading having -fallen at her
side were evidences of accident. â¢ It was
supposed 'that \ the \ light was ' blown , out
by the draught coming!. through. the open
window. .The next morning. Mr. ; Dunphy
visited Miss Wilbur with an intent to es
cort her to Oakland, where she was going
to attend the funeral of a friend. J
Baffled Medical Skill. . ; .
From February 16, 1898, to the time, of
her. death over forty physicians had- been;
called In to attend Miss Wilbur at. one
tlmeor. other. Work as they would, pre-,
scribing every treatment known. to. medU
cal science,, their efforts were of no ."avail.
Specialists with reputations, national in
character took charge of Miss Wilbur; but
success did' not follow. .Varying , diag
noses were made, each physician being al
lowed a sufficient, time to prove or dis
prove the worth of his prescribed - treat
ment. Â»â¢Â» â¢ :*.: * . - .Â¦â¢Â¦'
.Although unconscious, the patient was
not : insensible to pain. When suffering
physical agony she gave way to a spasm
of weeping and when relief came she was
instantly quiet and composed. During, all
this time of unconsciousness Miss Wilbur
was without the power of locomotion or
muscular articulation. At times there
were no indications in her face of- her
great mental disorder. Her eyes were
bright,.' her features composed and the>
expression that marked hqr countenance
was; that of a being with mind and.rea
son. Then 'there would come a sudden
change, and she would lapse Into a par
alytic condition.
' ' During the day the eyes were open at
will.' Nourishment, which was always in
.liquid form,' was administered through a
â¢ tube, that ran into the aesophagus. Until
' two days ago, when Miss Wilbur began to
weaken, she was apparently in compara
tively, good '; health.
She Never Waked.
i The story -that Miss Wilbur regained
consciousness a few days ago and uttered
in a ; semi-delirium, "Nobody to love me,"
,1s emphatically denied by all persons who
have been in constant attention since she
was stricken down. . . â¢

ing, no arrangements other than the time
set- having .been made. A- score or.two

em Part of the State.'. /.

'clsco northward,' and has added .15 ofian.
Inch of water to the already generous, wet

â¢ In the mountains snow Is now. falling

to the irrigation of the faf ms lying at the
foot of the hill slopes. 1 â¢- â¢ Â¦ â¢ â¢:*.'-'

urday and Sunday; yet the ranchers in the

Special Dispatch to The Call.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. WELLING
TOX" HOTEL, WASHINGTON, March 6.â
The Senate to-day passed a joint resolu
tion which will preserve the big trees In
Sequoia' Park. Representative de Vries
willTay it before President McKinley to
morrow for his signature.
Senator Perkins called up the Joint reso
lution, which had come over from the
House yesterday, and briefly explained its
purpose to his colleagues. Senator Hoar
suggested fnformally to. Perkins -the pro
priety of an amendment authorizing the
Secretary of the Interior to begin con
demnation proceedings at -once, but Per
kins assured the Senator from Massachu
setts that he was convincedjthat negotia-
Uons for the possession',, of the' gropes
would be amicable. â¢ If,' howeVer, ' con
demnation proceedings- became necessary
the measure as passed by' the House made
ample provisions for this contingency.
Hoar then expressed himself as perfectly
satisfied and, so the -resolution passed.'
Representative de Vries has invited Mrs.
Sharon of California, who Is here in the
interest of this measure as.the representa
tive of the ladies' club, to accompany him
to the White House to-morrow morning
that she may be present when President
McKinley signs the resolution, in which
the women of California have taken so
deep an interest. He has purchased a
gold pen, fitted into a handsome holder
for the use of the President, and after the
executive signature Is attached' he* wilt
present it to Mrs. Sharon for the ladles'
club.

Hayes of Cleveland, 0., for Vice Presi

It Is proposed that the Socialist Labor

For Yolo's County Fair.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
"WOODLAND, March 6.â The board of
directors of the Tolo County Agricultural
Association has appointed committees on
premium, finance and programme. There
will be liberal premiums offered for all
kinds of fruits, cereals, cattle," horses and

that it will be one of the greatest displays

directors are - unanimous In their opinion
that the Industrial exhibition should be
the principal feature of the fair.
CIRCULATED CANARDS
TO AFFECT STOCKS
Six Men Indicted for a Peculiar Crime
. by a New York Special Grand
Jury.
NEW YORK. March Câ The special
Grand Jury which has been investigating
Prr>r>klvn Rapid Transit mattÂ»js for the
past month to-day brought In a present
ment before Justice Fursman in the crim
inal term of the Supreme Court, together
with indictments against six men, mak
ing them in part responsible for the false
reports that had been circulated about
Brooklyn Rapid Transit, with a resulting
depression of the stock. The men indicted
are Alfred R. Goslin, who was arrested
m Philadelphia: Harry .T. Alexander, an
advertising ngent: Charles T. Davis, edi
tor of the Wall Street Review, and Eu
gene 1.,. Packer, all three of whom were
put under arrest In this city: Warner L.
Allen, an alleged stockholder of the
Brooklyn Rapid Transit Company, and
one Bogart. who Is said to be Goslin's pri
vate secretary.
The presentment of the Grand Jury In
timates there are others implicated In the
circulation. of false reports, although suf
ficient proof against them had not been
submitted to the jury.
Davis was arraigned on three indict
ments, two alleging felony and one mis
demeanor, and was held in $11,000 ball.
Packer was held in $J'YK) on two indict
ments, charging misdemeanor. Alexan
der was held In $7000 bail on three indict
ments, charging felony and conspiracy.
Ooslin has not been brought to this city
from Philadelphia.
Alexander and Davis were committed to
the Tombs, being unable to furnish a
bond.

furnish power to the numerous mining
and smelting plants of the county and to
transmit power and light to Redding.
The enterprise is backed by ample capi
tal and le already in possession of enough
advance contracts to guarantee financial
security. The plant will be located Just
outside the Government reservation at
Balrd, twenty-two miles from Redding
Fifty thousand inches of water from the
McCloud River have been appropriated.
The water will be conveyed In a canal
twelve miles long to the generating plant
situated Just above the United States
fishery. .
An electric road connecting Redding and
KeswlrV. is a possibility of the future.
The plant will be constructed with a ca
pacity of 4000 horsepower. The canal will
be built for 10,000-horsepower capacity, so
that the plsJit can be increased upon de
mand.
PROMINENT MERCHANT
ATTEMPTS SUICIDE
John H. Harpham of College City,
While Temporarily Insane, Tries
to Drown Himself.
Special Dispatch to The CalL
COLUSA. March 6.â John H. Harpham,
a merchant of College City, attempted to
ncommit EUicide Monday night while ter-
porarily lneane by throwing himself Into
the Sacramento riVer. Mr. Harpham left
his home some time In the. night and left
a letter to his wife to' the effect that she
would never see him again alive, a search
was immediately instituted and Mr. Harp
ham was traced from College City 10
Grand Island, twelve miles distance, on
the Sacramento river. Tracks led down
to the river edge and preparations were
being made to drag the river, when one
of the searchers happened to go Into an
old barn, and there lying partly hidden Jn
the hay was Harpham, his clothes wring
ing wet and nearly frozen to death. He.
was at once cared for, but his mind
seemed a blank. As the day passed his
reason seemed to gradually return and
great hopes are now entertained for his
ppec-dy recovery.

Father Yorke Discusses Vice
of Divorce and Duties
of Matrimony.
â¢ A most Interesting and elevating address
was delivered last evening at St. Mary's
Cathedral by Rev. Peter C. Yorke. His
subject was "Marriage and Divorce." The
lecture was listened to attentively by an
audience that filled the auditorium of the
big cathedral and overflowed into the gal
leries. In his discourse the eloquent
speaker drew the conclusions that every
family should be Independent within it
self; that the marriage contract should
be between but one man and one woman,
and that it should last until the termina
tion of the life of at least one of the par
ties thereto. Briefly, Father Yorke said:
"Man Is composed of three elementsâ
the body, the soul and the spirit. Between
the body and the soul is a continual con
flictâthe conflict between the higher and
the lower, man. The soul . desires those
things which the body does not.; To-night
I â will discuss the man as a member, of
the family and. the conflict which exists
i within him between the'body.on one side
and the spirit on the other. Naturally,
we would expect to find the soul on ' the
spirit's side. Theoretically, : It is on . the
side of crae*. but practically It is on the
.-".- :- â¢
ADVEHTISEMENTS.
veys. is brought Into the world in the
most helpless condition; and is the slow
est of all ; the members of the .animal
kingdom In reaching his maturity. Hence
the marriage contract should not be fleet
ing, but should be stayed. If It Is entered
Into for the good of the children. It should
last as long as they, do and exist through
out the whole human life. The contract
is not only for the man and the woman
who enter into it, but for the helpless
little ones they bring Into the world.
America's Greatest Vice.
"Polygamy is the first revolt of the
body against the unity of the family." And
then there is the polygamy on the Install
ment plan, which becomes possible by
reason of our divorce laws."
Father Yorke made a scathing refer
ence to the childless couples. "This is a
vice," he said, "in which man and woman
defeat' the ends -for which the marriage
state was designed and live only for their
own pleasures. The Roman ; empire was
rotted out by just such vice." He said
the distaste for children was spreading at
an alarminig rate, until it had become
"America's greatest vice."
"The Episcopalians have' tried to stop
divorce by, urging society to frown upon
divorced people," he continued. . "Society
cannot rise above Its source. Some class
might close its doors against the divorced,
but you will find enough divorced people
to form a society of their own. The mis
ery, that divorce essays to heal la a mis
ery that grows upon us. , - *^
"Unless we have true men and true wo."
men. we cannot, have a great nation. The
polygamy of the Mormon and the divorce
of the American must be destroyed If we
expect; the upbuilding of the , Christian
home and the sustaining of the nation."
This evening Father Yorke â¢ will discuss
"The Ethics of Education." â¢

Exceeded His Powers.
The Superior Court of Alaroeda received
a decided setback yesterday at the hands
of the Justices of the Supreme Court. I*
V. Murdock, a conductor on the Oakland.
San Lcandro & Haywards Railway, sued
the company for personal damages and
on the hearing he introduced, evidence to
show that the car on which. the accident
occurred was not safe and that he had so
informed the officials. The lower court
granted the company a nonsuit on the
ground that the evidence was not suffi
cient to warrant action. The higher court
reversed the order, stating that the jury
and not the court should be the ludga
of the facts and evidence.

Society's First Form.
"We are all dependent one upon the
other. It is not well for man to be alone.
In all walks of life we are compelled to
depend upon the exertions of our fellows,
and the more complex civilization grows
the greater becomes this dependency.
"Social instinct in man is naturalâ Jt la
part of his makeup. His happiness de
pends upon how much he can make others
contribute to his wants. Society has two
forms, the tamlly and the civil state. Tha
first form societj; takes is the family.
Both exist on their own merits; both are
independent of the other.
"There is much loose speaking and much
loose thinking about marriage. It is a
contract. Sometimes we read in the news
papers that it is a contract, and again
that it is something that should be. done
in the 'good old Scotch Presbyterian way.'
"The duty which devolves upon those'
Â¦who enter Into the marriage relation Is to
rear . families and bring up their children
In the proper way. The old : maxhn ' that
'every man's home Is his castle' â¢ empha
sizes the fact that the first necessity. of
every family is to be* independent; one
not to interfere with the other."

When the hearing of Mrs. Craven-Fair's
petition for a family allowance from the
Fair estate was resumed before Judge
Troutt- yesterday morning Mrs. Cosack
was put on the stand for further cross
examination by Attorney Pierson. While
she adhered to her original story, the law
yer drew from her the unexpected infor
mation that previous to the time when
she made her deposition in the case of
Angus vs. Craven she had an interview
Â¦with D. M. Delmas, In which he. advised
her to conceal anything she knew regard
ing the meeting of Senator Fair and' Mrs.
Craven at the Mission Grammar School
on the occasion when she was shown the
marriage contract.
She acknowledged that she followed in
structions, although she had sworn to tell
the truth and the whole truth.
She resented the insinuation that she
A Pair of Mrs. Craven-Fair's Prize Witnesses.
The Attorney, Upon Arrival From
Mexico, Flees to His Home in
Fear of Sylva and the Fairs.
LOUIS F. DUXAXD. the attorney
upon whom Mrs. Craven-Fair
placed v so much reliance to back
up her story of the Sausalito cere
mony and to shatter the conspiracy yarn
of Adolph Sylva, has returned from Mex
ico and Bays he is prepared'to substanti
ate the statements of the alltged widow
v{ the late Senator Fair.
He arrived at his home at San Rafael
Â«"arly yesterday . morning, accompanied
by City Attorney Thomas P. Boyd.
Boyd met Dunand on the Aca-
Jiulco yesterday morning and in a
launch the two evaded all inquiries and
went to Tiburon. where they caught the
Crrt train to San Rafael. Boyd says that
Mrs. Dunand requested him to meet her
husband, as she was extremely nervous
and apprehensive that Dunand. might fall
into the hands of Adolph Sylva or the Fair
heirs. Dunand, on his arrival at San Ra
fael, went straight to his hoiue and de
tied himself to visitors until late last
evening. When questioned in regard to
his Mexican trip Dunand ridiculed the
Idea that his Journey had any connection
with the Cravea-Fair case.
"My trip was purely a business one,"
he said, "made In the interest of a local
construction company. It had nothing
whatever to do with securing testimony."
Â¦While admitting the existence of con
tracts between Mrs. Craven-Fair and him
self. Dunand professes Ignorance as to the
particular one to which she referred In
her testimony.
"There are several documents existing
between Mrs. Fair and myself," he stat
ed. "It would not be. fair to her to di
vulge the contents of any of them until
after a consultation with her attorneys."
Dunand gave the following account of
his transactions with Mrs. Craven-Fair:
"I want it to be understood," he said,
â¢that our relations from first to last have
bten purely friendly. The first time I
went to Sausalito with her I was sent by
Mr. Foote. She stated to him that she
had be-en married to Senator Fair in Sau-
Falito by a justice whose name she had
forgotten. I believed her story to be
true."
"Do you believe In it still?" was asked.
"I do implicitly," he replied. "I intro
duced Mrs. Craven-Fair to Adolph Sylva
and there my connection with the case
practically ended. Within the next day
or two Sylva and Mrs. Craven-Fair met
In my office. Sylva said that if he was to
act in Mrs. Fair's interest he must have
a retaining fee. She gave him an order
drawn on Daniel Meyer for $200. I had
â¢several interviews with her and Sylva af
ter that, but nothing was said having any
bearing on the present case. I cannot say
more now. but when I am called upon to
testify I will tell the whole truth," con
tinued Dunand.
"How will that affect Mrs. Craven-
Fair's testimony?" was asked.
"I beiieve that my (statements will cor
roborate her story In every. essential par
ticular," he replied.
When questioned as to his opinion of
Adolph Sylva's pose as a champion of
truth and exposer of conspiracies, Dunand
Fhrugged his shculders and remarked that
"Sylva was a poor judge of the perspicu
ity of human nature."
A "dark horse" in the case Is Walter A.
Frost of New York, who traveled up on
th<9 steamer with Dunand from Panama.
Just wnat Frost's business Is is a mystery,
but he is supposed to be Interested in thÂ«
case as one of thÂ« witnesses to substan
tiate Sylva's etory. Sylva denied abso
lutely all knowledge of the man. Dunand
cays that he noticed Frost on the boat,
but had no conversation with him and
does not know what became of him after
the steamer's arrival.

Society Stunned When Brave Addison,
in Revenge, Spat Into the Plebeian
Beverage of the Un resentful
Mardi-Gras Leader.
could ;not shave done a : piece of
work. Then Mizner .turned one glance of
ferocity on the leader of. the Four Hun
dred and majestically strode away.
Greenway was * paralyzed. Recovering
his presence of mind he ordered more bter
in another glass. The onlookersâ Parrish.
Runyon and othersâ expected that the
hurry-up wagon would soon be summoned,
but the expected did not happen. Green
way mused, as only one accustomed to
musing can muse, land then went outsidu
and drank in a large breath of fresh air.
Then he- exclaimed. "The little puppy,"
and vanished -n the gloaming.
Meanwhile Mizner awaited results. Hold
ing in reserve his temper and saliva, ho
was ready to meet the shock of conflict.
He is still waiting.
Various are the comments made by the
Four Hundred. In one set of the smart
people it is contended that Greernvay
should have waited until Mizner ordered
a drink and then retaliated by spitting in
the beverage provided for quenching the
Mizner thirst. Others maintain that
Greenway, as the leader of a wine agency,
could not afford to resent an Insult placed
on > beer, and that the society leader de
served the rebuke for drinking beer in
stead of wine. , . . Â¦
The beauty of the afterglow of the car
nival is marred by these conflicting' opin
ions. '.The code does not state explicitly
what action a wine drinker should pursue
when he is insulted in the act of drinking
beer, hence society has something to talk
about and something to suggest. After
Lent the code of ethics may be amended.

SHIP SOLHEIM SIGHTED
IN FLAMES AT SEA
No Trace of the Captain and Crew
of an "Unfortunate. Nor
wegian Vessel. . -
PHILADELPHIA, March 6.-The Amer
ican line steamer Rhynland arrived to
day from Liverpool . and reported having
sighted the Norwegian ship Sclheim, lum
ber laden, from Mobile January 22, -for
Fleetwood, England, abandoned and on
fire. The Rhynland sighted the burning
ship on February 27, 'in latitude 45.49, long
itude 35.11. The American liner went to
the vessel to take off any who might be
aboard, but found no signs of life The
fire was burning fiercely from the stern to
midships. All .the sails of the ship had
either been scorched or burned.
The Solheim is supposed to have car
ried a crew of from' fifteen to twenty men.
Her captain's name was Metson.

TRADE TREATY

Commissioner Kasson Is
Now Considering the
Subject."
However, as the Measure Would Fol
low the Lines "of the French -
Treaty, It Seems Doomed
to Defeat. "<
Special Dispatch to The Call.'
CALL HEADQUARTERS, WELLING
TON HOTEL, WASHINGTON, March G.^-
It Is. understood that Mr. Kassor,,. the
Commissioner whom President McKinley
has designated to prepare reciprocal trade
arrangements with different foreign coun
triesunder authority conferred by section
3 of the Dingley act, is making prelim
inary studies of the Italian exports to this
country with a view to drawing upan
Italian reciprocal trade^rrangement. Mr.
Kasson's efforts with his trade arrange
ments In the past have met with no suc
cess. His latest tattempt with France is
conceded on every hand to be "doomed to
failure, and as the articles of trade In
volved in an Italian reciprocity treaty
would be closely similar to those in the
French and Jamaican treaties there 1c no
reason to believe that the Italian ar
rangement will escape the same fate .
. The members of the California delega
tion have been rt-celvlng telegrams urging
them to continue the vigorous opposition
which has defeated other treaties hostile
to California's Interests, and there is no
doubt that the Italian treaty will be pig
eonholed-in Mr. Kasson's desk along -with
its predecessors. ; Â¦ '- r
Senator, Perkins has discovered a prece
dent which has an Important bearing on
the disputed point of the right of tha
House to;pass on these. reciprocal. treaties.
It has been maintained that these trade
arrangements, .being in. the nature ' of
treaties, require only ratification by the
Senate. The House has conceded that
Fince these treaties all affect' the revenues
of this country, and 'not merely the for
eign policies, the .House must act jointly
with the Senate,' because under the consti
tution all revenue legislation must have
their co-operation. Senator Perkins -has
found that when' the question of abro
gating the Hawaiian treaty came up In
the Senate. Senator -MoitM. then chair
man of the Finance Committee of the Sen
ate, demanded that the bill be referred to
his committee,. because It affected the ie.v
enucs of this country.: A point of order
was made that the proper reference of the
Mil was to the Committee Qn> Foreign Ro
latoins, but the president of the Senate
ruled In favor of Mr..Morrlll. While, it'ia
true that the Senate, by a'majority -vote,
referred the treaty to the Foreign Rela
tions Committee, principally on account- of
Senator Morrill's well-known hostility* to
Hawaiian annexation, â¢ the- ruling- of * the
presiding officer established- a' precedent,
to the effect that treaties r affecting Ihe
revenues of this country are subject tp the
control of committees having jurisdiction
over the revenues. â¢ Senator -Perkins holds
that this -, the rlght-of ; the
House to demand a. voice In the settlement
of trade treaties... ."; Â¦ V. Â¦ .-: i-. ..

James B. Sovereign Describes Scenes

"WASHINGTON. March 6.â Before the
Coeur d'Alene investigation was resumed
to-day the House Military Committee held
an executive session , and decided not to
call upon the President for affidavits fi!Â«d
With him relative to the. conditions of the
mining country under ma'rtlal law. This
decision was due to some question as to
the right to make such requests -of tha
President, and, more particularly, because
the affidavits were not considered legal
evidence. :
â¢Andrew Devlin, a miner, was the first
witness to-day. His evidence was directed
to showing that orderly conditions.pre
vailed and that the local authorities could
have dealt with the situation' without re
sort to martial law. Â¦ '/ '
' James K. . Sovereign, former master
workman of the Knights of I^abor, sa'<l
he was in the Coeur d'Alene district dur
ing the demonstration against the Bunker
Hill mines and was editor of the official
organ of the miners' organization at Wal
lace, Idaho. . He attended a labor meeting
at Gem, Idaho, the night before the blow
ing up of the mill; and later that .night
attended a meeting at Wallace, but there
was no talk of blowing up the mill. He
never heard 'that any demonstration was
contemplated against the mining property.
The next morning, while standing in front
of. his office a man on horseback rodo by
and said! - â¢ '
" "Sovereign, they are coming."
V He . asked j who was . coming, and the
horseman answered:
"The Canyon Creekers."
Many Men With Winchesters,
-. He saw a train with a number of men
aboard going down to Wardner. There
were conflicting rumors of what would oc
cur at.Wardper. He went down on the
noon train and reached Wardner. in time
to hear the thrse explosions when the mill
was blown up. He saw many men, some
with Winchesters, and there was consider
able > fighting. To avoid the conflict h'j
stepped upon an engine. . While on th*Â»
engine a man with a revolver boarded the
cab and demanded that the train Ibe
brought to a stop,- and this was don*.
Meantime there Â¦, was some shooting into
the flume of <he mining camp. The train
then proceeded to' Wallace. There was
no excitement there. The same quiet pre
vailed at Wardner, Kellogg and other
points. 'Â¦' Â¦ Â¦Â¦ - -.. Â¦ â¢
Sovereign told of his appearance before
the Coroner, when many soldiers and sev
eral lawyers identified with the mining
interests were present. As the military
was in control; Sovereign applied to mili
tar.y headquarters for a pass.. The wit
ness said he told the major in command
that he was president of a Montana min
ing company. Thereupon the officer
showed excessive affability and said the
military forces proposed to take care of
the mine operators. He heard he was to
be arrested, but this never occurred.
Sovereign said he had advised that the
soldiers be used to assist the local author
ities, but not to enforce martial law. In
stead of that, martial law was enforced
with extreme severity. The conditions
were less acute, he said, than in Chicago
during the great strike, and yet the mili
tary system adopted was far more rigid.
Insolence of Negro.Soldiers.
â¢ Sovereign described scenes, $t .the .Vbull
pen.'-' : He" said Â¦he I heard i negro, soldiers
use obscene language against the Impris
oned, miners. . When ' tha , man were ar r
rested they 'wore light clothwiÃ, such as
Is used in deep, mines, and, although the
weather was very cold, they.' received no
additional clothing. ' " â¢
The witness stated that while one. of
the miners was in the "bullpen," a. per
son attempted to force an entrance to the
room pf the miner's wife. The man com
mitting the offense was arrested by a
local constable, but afterward released by
the soldiers. Â¦ When asked who was the
responsible- official in charge of affairs.
Sovereign* said there was no difference
between - Governor Steunenberg, Gen
eral Merriam, Captain Edwards and Au
ditor Sinclair. 3 . . *
"They all had Krag-Jorgensens behind
them," he declared. '
He added that one of the mine officials
had given the order to "fire" at the time
the demented miner, Johnson, was shot.

LITTLE Bennle Laws, 13 years
old, walked in his sleep yester
day morning and plunged to
his death through a window
while pursuing an imaginary enemy.
His mother, seeing his danger, had
tried to catch him and for a brief
moment had hold of his heel as he
was falling out of the window, but
the foot slipped from her grasp and
he was dashed to death In the yard
below.
Mrs. Lillian Laws resides at 226 De
visadero street, where the tragedy oc
curred. With her were her son, Ben
jamin H. Laws, and her daughter, her
husband being absent in the East.
The boy was a victim of dyspepsia,
and this disease brought on insom
nia, but he was never known to walk
in his sleep. " At 1 o'clock yesterday
morning Mrs. Laws, whose slumbers
were light, was awakened by the
sound of the boy running around the
rooms. She got out of bed and found
him in the hall pursuing -an Imagi
nary burglar and crying "Stop him,
stop him! Don't let him get away!"
The peculiar sound -of the child's
voice and his actions convinced his
mother. that he was in a somnambu
listic state, and she ran after him to
awaken him and prevent him from
hurting himself. At that moment he
ran into his own room and, vaulting
over his bed, jumped through the
window. His distracted mother threw
herself desperately forward , and
caught hold of one of his heels as he
was disappearing, but it slipped out
of her grasp, and he fell to the yard
twenty feet below. In his fall he
struck the kitchen porch and then the
ground.
M. T. Moses, an uncle of the boy,
picked up the mangled and uncon
scious form and carried it upstairs.
Medical aid was summoned, but the
poor little fellow was past all cure.
He had one moment of conscious
ness, and when he looked up at his
weeping mother and she saw that he
recognized uer she asked him to put
his arms around her neck. With the
last remnant of his fast ebbing
strength he twined his arms about his
mother's neck, laid his head upon her
bosom and smiled into her face. Then
he closed his eyes and passed away.

so far to encourage them in the belief
that their demands will be acceded, to
by the management in San Francisco.
They are. in fact, thoroughly satisfied
that their complaints will be sent East
for settlement. In that event
must elapse before they secure a final an
swer.
Sufficient ha? been made public through
one source and another to establish the
fact that the organized departments of
labor In the railroad service are unani
mously opposed to Huntington's insur
ance proposition. The brotherhoods have
completed their plans for concerted action
in opposition to tho bureau. The next
step, so far as the Western division ope
rators are concerned, has been definitely
formulated. The brotherhoods, it is un
derstood, propose to make a campaign
among the unorganized employes. These
Include the shopmen, mechanics, laborers,
car repairers, car cleaners and the like
who have not been banded together since
the American Railway Union was dis
solved. These men are in a position to
take individual stands for or against the
bureau, because they have no ties of
federation to bind them to concerted
moves.
The argument which appeals to mÂ»n in
the brotherhoods has not the same weight
with these outside employes. The broth
erhoods stand firm in the belief that their
organized existence is jeopardized by this
company insurance plan. Certain It is
they declare that under the provisions of
the* scheme no new employe who is com
pelled to join the Insurance bureau will
feel like affiliating with a brotherhood
which is fundamentally an insurance or-
ganizatlon. The argument the brother
hood men make is that they will be de
prived of the opportunity of Increasing
their membership, and that it will be only
a matter of time when the brotherhoods
will die.; - s -.--"Â¦
Not a few of the subordinate officials
have been approached by the men since
the new scheme was promulgated, but
none of them has been able to overcome
that chief objection to the plan.
Every feature of tho Insurance plan is
being earefuly discussed at the brother
hoods* meetings, that the special commit
tee which is to appear before Genorol
Manaper Kruttschnitt next week will be
full}' fortified to explain the position of
the men. There is hardly a clause In vhe
scheme that meets the approval of the af
fected employes. They see between the
lines the hand of coercion. \VhilÂ»
on the surface there is no
suggestion of compulsion so far
as. old employes are concerned, the
men are so thoroughly satisfied that re.
fusalto join will eventually result in loss
of position that they take no stock what
ever in the voluntary aspect of the situ
ation. It is compulsory that all men en
tering- the service of the company afttr
March 1 must join the relief bureau. The
chief difficulty the men have to contend
against in making- a united front Is the
possible attitude of the unorganized em
ployes. But to overcome this no oppoitu
nity Is being lost to show those workmen
that their interests do not Up in any other
direction than that pointed out by the
brotherhoods.
There is no disposition on the part of the
engineers, conductors, firemen anrl other
train men to unduly magnify the danger

WALKED IN HIS SLEEP
AND PLUNGED TO DEATH
THE EMPORIUM.
THE EMPORIUM.
THE EMPORIUM.
EXTRA POLICE
TO WATCH THE
HIGHBINDERS
Authorities Endeavoring to
Suppress Further War
Among Chinese.
Fighting Tongs Have Not Yet Slaked
Their Thirst for Revenge and
More Trouble Is Ex
pected.
Chinatown is in a ferment and the high
binders have only pocketed ] their guns
and sheathed â¢ their knives until some
fresh imaginary . wrong that demands
expiation by blood lays hold of them. The
complication of the tongs is a veritable
Chinese puzzle, but this time both branch
associations of the See Yups and
Yups are dove-tailed, prohibiting the pos
sibility of a big trade war between these
two societies. '* . .
The murder of two fish merchants Mon
day morning may or may not settle the
present score. Both -men were members
of the Sam Yup Company, but not at
tached to any of the warring tongs. Just
what new alliances will be. made among
the tongs and what blood must be let to
satisfy the highbinders' fury for revenge
may be determined at any day.
An arrest was made yesterday morning
by Detective Ed. Gibson of a Chinese said
to be one of the murderers who shot down
the two merchants. His name is Chang
Chung, but all connection with the event
is emphatically denied by him.
The Police Department is formulating
various schemes to prevent future China
town outrages. Chief Sullivan has de
tailed an extra squad of policemen for
duty in the turbulent quarter. Sergeant
Pat Mahoney has been put in charge. Un
der him will be Patrolmen Mackey, Daly,
Driscoll and Behan, all of whom are stal
wart officers. Sergeant Mahoney was
specially selected because of his rcputa
â¢tion as a silencer of hpodlumism. The
squad will attend to highbinders exclu
sively, assisted by Detective Ed Gibson.
Dong Gong, interpreter in the police
courts, and the man who started a late
highbinder trouble, declares that the ven
geance wreaked upon the two merchants
Monday morning was originally meant for
Dong Gong and his family. The Suey
Sings have laid $500 blood money upon
Dong Gong, he says, and he is ; yet wait
ing.
Consul Ho Vow is of the opinion that
international agreement will alone settle
the differences between the highbinder so
cieties, unless the Chief of Police in
creases the force in Chinatown and beats
down lawlessness by force of arms.
of serious trouble. But many of them rio
not hesitaie to declare that there will be
no stopping until some favorable result is
attained. Not since ISS4 have the men
been so determined to assert their side of
the controversy with the company. ' .
Evidence of the care which Is beinÂ«;
taken by the men to maintain a dignified
yet strong position is manifested in their
refusal to furnish information concerning
the executive meetings which are being
held this week by the various brother
hoods. Orders have been given that the
members shall not publicly discuss the In
surance scheme nor any of the objections
they have *to it. Every move has been
made primarily to organize a represenia
the grievance ermmlttee, which shall be
equipped to make a proper showing beroro
the railroad officials.
If failure shall result then another pro
gramme of attack on the scheme will bo
inaugurated. Whether this will culminate
in a strike is a matter for future develop
ments to determine, t
Conforming to the plan arranged, the
special committee of' the Brotherhood of
Railway Trainmen and the Order of Rail
way Conductors met this evening at West
Oakland with E. C. Fellows Lodge No. 71.
Brotherhood of Locomotive Firemen. The
firemen appointed a committee similar to
that of the other organizations. This
leaves only the engineers to name their
representatives for the joint meeting,
which will be held Saturday evening As
soon as. the union grievance committee

arrange upon their plan for presenting the
objections of the brotherhoods to General

SCHEME THAT
MADE MEYER
COMMISSIONER
Southern Pacific Relief De
partment Demanded It
of Gage.
Possible Opposition by the Leader of
Brotherhood of locomotive En
gineers Thus Done Away
With.
When the Southern Pacific Company
conceived the new relief department it
did so with a prescience of possible oppo
elUon on the part of the railroad em
ployes. Long before the plans were form
ulated the first step was taken and Gov
ernor Henry T. Gage was called on to
render his assistance. Gage's helpful
Ji* 3^ as raJsed b y the appointment of
iÂ» ' ileVM " as Labor Commissioner.
Meyer was the most formidable man the
railroad company had met in its dealings
with the brotherhood associations, lie
was at the head of the Brotherhood of
l^ocomotive Engineers, the strongest so
ciety of laboring men that came In
active and daily contact with the govern
ing powers of the yellow building. For
tight years Meyer represented the loco
motive engineers with unfaltering zeal.
He knew their power and their influence
and was at all times the champion of their
cause whenever disputes that needed set
tlement arose or differences had to be pet
tied That the railroad company respected
Merer s power and Influence was ' dis
p!aj-ed on more than one occasion.
With the idea of a relief bureau in mind
the Southern Pacific master minds can
vassed the entire field. They scheduled
anc tabulated the obstacles that stood In
the way of ultimate success. H. V. Meyer
was the greatest possible obstruction to
the successful consummation of their
ecfceme, so they found a place which they
theught he might accept- The Southern
Pacific Induced Gage to make the appoint
ment and the strongest man in the broth
erhood organization took the bait. Mey
er's successor in the Brotherhood of Loco
motive Engineers has neither his experi
ence nor influence.
When Meyer received his appointment
there was a batch of applicants for the
position. The Governor had previously
anr.ounced that he would select a man
froai the ranks of labor, and the race for
the appointment was hotly contested. At
the last moment Meyer's candidacy was
announced, to the .utter surprise of all
other aspirants. Without any ado or hesl
tarcy Gage made him his choice and now
thÂ« Southern Pacific relief department
coiceivers simile to think they have de
pr.ved the men who might make an or
ganized stand against the scheme of their
nnst influential leader.

THE CHRISTIAN'S POWER.
Rev. Philip Coombe spoke beforÂ© the
Congregational ministers at their " meet-
Ing on Monday, his subject beinfl "The
Christian's Power Over Opposing Forces."
In part he said:
We are living in an age in which the forces
which arise to arrest the progress of truth are
everywhere in evidence and strikingly in ar
ray. Many Christians, both ministers and lay
men, oftentimes allow thesÂ« adversaries to ex
ert i too (treat an influence over them. In this
materialistic age we are too prone to let that

OUR TRADE WITH THE ORIENT.
IZIJROM the Department of Agriculture there has
1 been issued a bulletin of statistics concerning our
trade with China, Japan and Hongkong for the
decade ending with 1899. The showing made of the
development of the trade is remarkable, for during the
ten years it increased from a total of $46,294,167 in
ISB9 to $87,305,688 during the last fiscal year.
Of the total amount of the traffic of 1809 the exports
from China, Japan and Hongkong to the United
States were valued at $47,815,035, while the imports
from this country were valued at $39,400,653. The
showing made by the statistics .is therefore not as
gratifying. as it might be. There are no natural rea
sons why our exports to the Orient should not be at
least equal, to the value of our imports, and there is
some satisfaction in noting that the tendency is in
that direction. Thus on comparing the exports of
1889 with those of ten years later it appears that the
value of our exportations to those countries increased
during the decade by 256 per cent, while the value of
our imports from them increased only, 36 per cent.
Detailed statistics showing the relative quantities
and values of the various exports during 1890, were
not available at the time of the preparation of the bul
letin, but in the previous year 40 per cent of the whole
were of agricultural products, while in 1889 only 25
per cent were of that character. The article which
figures chiefly in the increase is wheat,' the shipments
of which to Japan alone increased from $300,609 in
1889 to $8,969,816 in 1898. , . .'
Taken as a whole the exporta of 1898 consisted
mainly of five leading articlesâ cotton, cotton manu
factures, kerosene oil, wheat flour and manufactures
of iron and, steel. Of these items it is significant that
the importations of raw cotton were- valued at over
$7,000,000, while the cotton manufactures were valued
at but little more than $63,000. It seems the orientaj
people are beginning to use our raw material and do
their own spinning. Â» ,
There is little question butjwhat.we could largely
increase our exports to China if we would follow the
policy which has enabled Germany to enter that mar
ket with .such , success against Great Britainâ
that of employing as Consuls men who can speak the
language 1 of the people. /and promoting steamship
lines to carry on the commerce. The Japanese and
the Chinese are our neighbors and should be . our
customers.; They will . be ko as soon as we enter ac
tively into the task of beating our European rivals by
the adoption of mercantile tactics as wise as their own.

LIGHT ON THE PHILIPPINES.
r\ YEAR ago the Filipino "rebellion" was over.
rA A few days after a Kansas soldier's gun precipi
* * tated hostilities, and after Torres, representing
Aguinaldo, had assured General Otis that there had
been no intention to bring on a war, that it was an ac
cident caused by retaliation of the first shot fired by
our side, and Otis had replied that war had begun
and must go on to the bitter end, official dispatches
from Manila announced that pacification had been ac
complished.
Reference to the files of the daily press prove this.
April 3, 1599, Otis cabled: "Present indications de
note insurgent government in perilous condition; its
army defeated, discouraged and scattered; insurgents
returning to their homes." June 1 Otis telegraphed
thct 30.000 soldiers will suffice for effective control of
the Philippines. August 12 the President announced
that Ajruinaldo was making his last play, and the war
would be over by November I. August 16 it was de
cided to send tp Otis 63.500 soldiers to pacify the
Philippines. December 12 it was officially announced
from Washington that rebellion no longer existed.
February i, 1900, one year after the war began,
.^iniinxldo not yet located: no surrender; r.o
decrease in weekly mortality lists among our
soldiers: losses in American army, 1485 killed. 1051
vounded. and Filipino hatred of us universal and
- ..Â¦â¢Â¦<Â¦Â» . Â¦ -:Â¦â¢-Â¦â¢â¢ - . -1
Lrmer than ever, in consequence of a year 01 slaughter
ar.d destruction.
March 3 Associated Press reports interview with
one of our military officers in Manila, that rebel army
has been wonderfully rehabilitated in the last month,
particularly in the northern provinces: that Filipinos
professedly friendly to us are officers in the wide
spread Masonic organization which dedicates itself to
independence. They are circulating Senator Beve
ridge's speech to prove that we mean simply an op
pressu'e commercial exploitation of the islands, and
an editorial from an administration newspaper in
Washington declaring the same thing.
Otis has sent for 1000 pairs of handcuffs to be used
on American soldiers who have been driven insane by
the climate.
March 5 General Wheeler arrives and reports a
banquet by the Sultan of Sulu and return festivities to
the polygamous Mahometan on board an American
nian-of-war. Colonel Barry says there remains but
a scattered fragment of Aguinaldo's forces. Telegrams
of same date trom Manila report three American ex
peditions against Filipino armies in Camarines and
Nueva Caceres. helped out by gunboats; two Ameri
cans killed, many wounded; 140 Filipinos killed;
Filipino soldiers attacking Americans every night,
and foreigners doing business compelled to live on
ships in the harbor of Legaspi, or in the American
barrack?. Same date Otis reports engagements along
San Miguel Bay: Adjutant Gallagher killed, eight
Americans wounded; same date an amigo led our
men into an ambush and killed Lieutenant Kohler; in
revenge our troops burned a town and slew twenty
four of the villagers. Same date transport Sheridan
sailed from Manila with a large number of convicts
and twenty insane soldiers.
The dullest man must be impressed by the record.
Another rainy season is at hand and the islands are
less pacified than a year ago, except the Sultan of
Sulu. who is reported by General Wheeler to be proud
to be under the stars and stripes with his harem and
his slaves. But at last light is breaking in this coun
try, and at the right point in the political sky. Presi
dent McKinley has appointed a new commission to
deal with the Philippine question. He chose first his
personal and political friend, Judge Taft of Cincin
nati. The Judge's friends banqueted him on the sth
tr.st. The banquet was presided over by Mr. M. E.
Ingalls. who announced himself an expansionist of the
most radical order. A speech followed by Judge
Taft's former law partner. Judge Harmon, who was
â¢Attorney General in Mr. Cleveland's last Cabinet.
Harmon brought a profound hush upon the company
by a carefully worded speech against expansion. In
its course one guest cried out, "There are only four
of you." But its conclusion was heartily applauded.
The last speech was made by Judge Taft himself, and
furnished the genuine surprise of the occasion. He
said: "The Philippine Commission is not yet organ
ised. Its members have not conferred together. I
speak therefore for myself alone. Like Judge Har
mon, and unlike Chairman Ingalls, I am not now and
never have been an expansionist. I have always
hoped that the. jurisdiction of our nation would not
extend beyond territory between the two oceans. We
have not solved all the problems of popular govern
ment so perfectly as to justify our voluntarily seeking
more difficult ones abroad."
So breaks the light. The sycophants, who have
mnde the mistake of thinking that President McKin
ley demanded disciples instead of advisers, will be ad
.monished by his appointment of Judge Taft that: he
proposes not to conclude public opinion on this sub
ject, but to go on the line to which The Call has ad
vised Congress to adhere, by letting legislation alone
as to our external possessions until the facts can all
be known, when a right policy will crystallize. It is
noteworthy that Mr. Ingalls. who presided at the Taft
banquet, is a Democrat. ' General Wheeler, another
Democrat, comes home from the Philippines inflam
ing American greed by putting commercial exploita
tion foremost as the only reason he has for conquest
of thf inlands. So he exclaims, in rapt admiration of
the almighty dollar, "It only requires American brains
and push to make the Philippine*, Guam and Sulus
the most productive islands on the face of the earth."
There are 'to be many international conferences , at
the Paris exposition, but. there will be no attempt to
duplicate the late gathering at the Hague even as a
side show. : v?
THE STOCKMEN ORGANIZE.
IT seems novel that an industry as important as the
livestock interest of the Pacific States has been, up
to this time, without organization. .
The Western tour of the Secretary of Agriculture
last summer was partly for the purpose of inspecting
the condition of the Western stock ranges on the arid
lands of the public domain. His practical eye saw at
once the deterioration of these land:, by destruction of
their natural forage crop, causea by overstocking
through the use' of them in common. He advised a
change in the method of grazing, and that the stock
range?, more valuable for that than any other present
! purpose, be protected by leaseholds, the title remain
ing in the Federal Government, and the lands being
so under Federal control that the Government can
compel the renewal of their forage and the restoration
of their primitive capacity for feeding stock. In the
beginning the stockmen were indifferent. Many^pre
| ferred to let things go on as they have been going, to
I let the forage crop be destroyed, and deciding that
as after them would come the desert and the drought
; they would get all they could off the common range
! for a few years and retire from the business. But the
impulse given to the subject by the Secretary of Ag
riculture was sufficient to induce the introduction of
several bills to effect the purpose he favored. Still the
policy of indifference" and delay was manifested by the
stockmen themselves. It will be remembered that
when the water storage convention met in this city
last November its whole purpose was held up by cer
tain influences which promised that the grazing lands
should be leased and the revenue therefrom, above the
cost of administration, should be given to the States*
and Territories in which the lands lie for use in stor
ing flood waters for irrigation. So "the increase of
irrigating water by preventing the flood waste came to
be impinged upon the leasehold policy for the grazing
lands.
Mr. Irish of California, while in Washington last
January, found the leasing question in abeyance, wait
ing for tfce stockmen to relax their indifference,
withdraw their opposition, or cease their policy of
delay. As he had promoted the water storage conven
tion and plan, he saw the necessity of getting the leas
ing policy in action, and to that end invited the stock
men of the Pacific States to the conference held in
this city on Monday. The results, so far, are grati
fying. All shades of opinion were represented at the
meeting. Many differences were composed by getting
together. A permanent organization was formed,
which already includes a membership in California,
Nevada, Arizona. New Mexico and Oregon. A me
morial oa the subject of the leaseholds was addressed
to Congress and ordered circulated in the arid re
gions, and all indifference was submerged in a most
active interest. Mr. Ifusk. the permanent president,
in declaring the proper features of a leasehold bill,
.^?id that no bill should pass that did not regard
equally the interests of the landholder, the agricultural
settler, present and future, the mineral prospector, the
small herdsman, and all the classes of citizens who
make up the sturdy frontier population of- the arid
regions, where the grazing lands lie. This sentiment
is fully expressed in the memorial. It was the general
iee!ing that the Foster bill, now before the Senate,
offtrs the best basis for the perfection of a satisfactory
measure, which should be passed by the present Con
gress. All agreed that no opportunity should be
offered by the law for the large meat-packing com
bines to get hold of the ranges, and that the lawless
ness that has too much characterized the use of
ranges in common would cease by permitting the
owners of all kinds of stock to lease ranges and then
compelling them to keep their livestock on their own
holdings.
The greatest benefit is likely to follow this organi
zation. Every stockman should be a,membcr of it. and
it shoulci not cease its activities until the lands con
cerned are protected against destruction of their for
age, the meat supply against decline, and the future
settler assured of a home on an agricultural holding,
wherever the possibility may appear.

WHERE THE GERMANS BEAT US.
T \EPORTS from Washington are to the effect
U-/ that the diplomatic and consular reform bill
has been shelved in committee and is not likely
to be taken up in the House at this session. On the
other hand, reports from Berlin announce that the
imperial Government has undertaken a thorough re
organization of the German consular service, with the
intention of making it even more effective than at
present.
It is to be borne in mind that the consular service
of Germany is already one of the best trained and ad
ministered commercial bureaus in the world. Through
ihe efficiency of their representatives abroad, the mer
chants and the manufacturers of the empire have rap
idly advanced to a leading rank among the great com
mercial nations. They are now rivals of Great Brit
ain in all parts of ; the globe, and are one of the
strongest competitors we will have to meet in our
efforts to expand our foreign trade. It is, then, a
matter of considerable concern to us that a movement
toward an improvement in the consular service is
dropped at Washington simultaneously with the un
dertaking of an enterprise toward that end at Berlin.
It may be conceded that our Consuls have done
good work in times past, but the times have changed,
and the class of men in our service has not changed
with them. In the days when our exports were made
up almost wholly of raw material or foodstuffs and
were shipped mainly to Great Britain, and.' when we
had no very active competition to meet, our system
of selecting Consuls did fairly well. Now, however,
we are shipping enormous quantities of manufac
tured goods and have come into direct rivalry with na
tions having a trained consular corps. We must, then,
have more competent representatives abroad than we
have had before. It is a necessity of the times!
The handicap upon our commerce resulting from
inefficient Consuls has been noted by the more inteU
ligent Chinese and Japanese themselves. In his re
cent address before the University of Pennsylvania
Wu Ting Fang. Chinese Minister at Washington,
said: JJ l \ must acknowledge that your diplomatic and
consular officers in China have thus far acquitted
themselves well, considering the -disadvantageous
position in which they are placed. Most of the
European Governments send young men to the East
to learn the language and study, the customs of the
country. After, a residence of two or. three years,
when they prove themselves proficient, after pass
ing a strict examination, they are placed in responsible
positions as student interpreters, consular assistants,
etc. Merit' is rewarded- by promotion. Thus those
Governments have competent men specially fitted for
service in the Orient.* It may not be unwise for your
Government to adopt a: similar system.'.' - -
, .We arc now building' up in the United States com-
The corporations and their tools who have been
fighting the Nicaragua canal bill all along are now
pretending to be very much opposed to the Hay
Pauncefote treaty. Any old thing that serves as a
brick to shy at the canal project suits them, and comes
hand}'. â¢

SPRINKLING OF
STREETS TO BE
AGAIN ARGUED
Merchants' Association About
to Consider Matter
Once More.
Supervisors May Be Asked to. Repeal
the Ordinance That Provides
for Keeping the Pave
ments Dry.
At tho next meeting of the board of
directors of the Merchants' Association a
petition to the board to exercise its influ
ence with the Supervisors to have the or
dinance repealed that was passed about a
year ago forbidding the sprinkling of the
bituminlzed streets in the city will be
taken up for consideration. The matter
has been brought to " the notice .of tho
Merchants' Association by several retail
merchants doing business -on Kearny
street. The ordinance was passed by tho
efforts of the Sflciety for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Animals, their reason being
that water caused the horses to slip.
Â¦ President Dohrmann of the Merchants'
Association said yesterday afternoon that
the retail merchants on Kearny street harl
quite generally asked for the influence of
the association to have the ordinance re
pealed, but that up to that time he had
not heard that any Market street mer
chants had moved in the matter. lie hod
no idea what action the directors of the
association were likely to take. The argu
ments, as- he summed them up pro arrl
con, were in substance that on one side
were the dangers to horses when the pavo
ments were sprinkled and also the sup
posed damage to pavements due to the use
of water, which was said to rot them. On
the other side the arguments were that
the dust in the absence of the sprinkling
of the pavements in the business sections
of the city damaged storks of goods of
all sorts; also that the dust was a sonrc*
of discomfort to residents and visitors ta
the city, and also might tend to proDa
gate disease. ".There were merchants who
wished to have .the existing conditions
changed, -because the city, had seemingly
taken a backward step in. the matter of
cleanliness in the most traveled and most
used thoroughfares. He had no personal
opinion to express, for he did not wish to
prejudge .. the case. Doubtless the . whole
subject would be discussed at the meeting
of the directors, for it had been placed
upon the regular programme for consider
ation.
The Society for the Prevention of Cru
elty to Animals will probably oppose the
repeal of the ordinance, or at least that
was the view of the matter taken by Sec
retary Holbrook last evening. He said
that he would like to be heard by the
Merchants' Association directors and
would probably request the privilege of a
hearing.

A SOUTHERN PACIFIC TRICK.
SOMETHING more than ordinary submissive
ness or stupidity would have been required on
the part of the employes of the Southern Pacific
Railroad to induce them to accept the new scheme of
"relief" without at least as much of a protest as men
dependent upon the corporation for work and wages
can afford to make. The scheme is an ingenious
combination of tricks to defraud the employes and
benefit the corporation. - ? x ,-*j" :
The most salient feature of the plan is the evident
design to break down Â¦ the various "brotherhoods"
which the employes have organized for mutual pro
tection and benefit. An important part of the work
of the brotherhoods is to provide relief for members
in case of sickness or accident. The funds for such
purposes are raised by an assessment upon the mem
bership, and it is, clear that if the members be now
forced to "voluntarily" join the corporation . relief
scheme and pay contributions to that fund, the
brotherhoods will be heavily handicapped and may
be even forced out of existence.
That, however, is but one of the tricks of the
scheme. Another hardly less important is to be found
in the clause by which all who sign the application fo/.
membership in the corporation relief plan release the
company from all claims for damages by reason of
injury or death. As the law stands the corporation
would have to pay such damages out of its own
funds, but b>' the operation of the new relief system
the payments to the injured man or to the family of
one who is killed are taken out of the funds contributed
by the employes. Thus the employes are to be made
to pay for any accident to one of them, although due
to the negligence of the corporation itself.
Collis Huntington has frequently boasted that no
one would ever track him through .the world by the
quarters he had dropped. It might be easy to iden
tify him, however, by the number of quarters found on
him that belong to other people. This latest scheme
of taxing out of the pockets of wage-earners the pay
ments which should be made by the corporation it
self is one of the meanest pieces of thrift of which
even Collis has ever been proud.
Mr. Hess, the gentleman whom Huntington sent
out from the East to organize the relief scheme "vol
untarily," has given a frank statement of the advan
tages which the widow and the fatherless will derive
from the scheme. He said if an employe of the rail
road should be killed and the widow sue for damages
and the court award them the corporation would not
pay. It would appeal from one court to another
court and prolong the litigation until lawyers' fees had
eaten up all that was due the widow. It is a plain
boast that the railroad can use the courts at its pleas
ure to voluntarily defeat justice, and from the boast
the argument is drawn that the employes would better
come into the scheme and consent to pay the dam
ages, for the railroad will not.
There was but one thing needed to cap the climax
of corporation tyranny and fraud, and it was promptly
forthcoming â Mr. Hess called the scheme "philan
thropy." . , - Â¦

"v^TTTHE LITERATURE OF PERSIA.
BY PROF. PAUL ELMER MORE.
In studying Persian literature It Is nec
essary first of all . to have some under
standing of the important historical
â¢changes through whicif the people of Iran
passed. Three strongly marked periods
may be observed: I. the ancient; 11. the
middle, and 111, the new. In the first
period we have records In two distinct
though closely related languages â the
Avestan, which will be the particular sub
ject of this paper, and the old Persian
contained In cuneiform Inscriptions on
stone extending from a few years after
the conquest of Babylon by Cyrus In 53!)
B. C. to the conquest of Alexander. From
the age of Alexander, when Greek influ
ence became predominant In Asia Minor,
until the Sassanian dynasty came to the
throne, in the third century, after Christ,
there is a complete blank in Persian rec
ords. With the Sassanian Kings the old
Magian priests regained their influence,
and the Zoroastrian religion became all
powerful In the state. The sacred books
in the ancient Avestan language became
the Bible of the land. But In the mean
while the language of the country had
suffered great changes, and the religious
commentaries, corresponding to the Pa
tristic literature of tne Christians, were
now composed in the Pahlavi, a tongue
which is related to old Persian much as
Italian is related to Latin. This body of
Pahlavi commentaries forms the middle
period. But Persian was doomed to un
dergo another and more far-reaching rev
olution. In the seventh century the coun
try was subdued by the Arabs, and hence
forth Mohammedanism became the re
ligion of the land. A number of the Per
sians escaped as colonists and settled In
India, where their descendants, the Par
sees, still live and maintain the ancient
Zoroastrian faith. Among them are pre
served the sacred Avesta and the later
Pahlavi books, although their spoken lan
guage has altered considerably since the
days of the Sassanids.
Meanwhile with the Arab conquest
Persia itself entered upon a new era. The
language developed into modern Persian
and was modified by a large Infusion of
Arabic words. The modern Persian lit
erature may be said to have begun about
the year 1000 A. D., with the Shah Xameh,
the great epic of Flrdusl. Intrinsically
and considered as pure literature modern
Persian, including such names as Firdusl,
Saadi, Omar Khayyam, Hatlz and others,
ranks easily first among Oriental litera
tures. The Persian inscriptions and the
Pahlavi books we must, for lack of space,
pass over unnoticed. To-day let us turn
for a moment to the Avesta, which con
tains the ancient religion of Zoroaster.
Of the two kindred people united under
Cyrus into one great empire the Medes
are the oldest In civilization, and it was
arr.ong them that the strange and little
understood religion of the Maglans de
veloped. The Magians themselves were
apparently a tribe of priestly inheritance,
very much like the Levltes among the
Jews. They were the learned men of the
East, familiar to us from the Bible, and if
for a time, under the sway of Persian and
Greek rulers, their authority was some
what diminished, they at last came to
their own under the Sassanian dynasty.
It is not easy to determine what was the
original form of the Magian religion, be
cause in the form in which It has come
down to us it was profoundly modified by
the influence of one dominant reformer.
In general, however. It must have been
largely akin to the polytheistic faith of
the Hindu Vedas, for. In fact, the Hindu
and Iranian religions were sisters derived
from a common mother.
But In the seventh century B. C. there
arose in Media a man of profound re
ligious instinct, a great prophet, who
wrought a revolution in the popular faltb.
From that day to -this the religion of the
Magians is called Zoroastrlanism, r.fter
his name. A few years ago it waa com
mon to speak of Zoroaster aa a mere
myth; Just as Buddha and Christ were
myths to a certain school of skeptlc3. But
reflection and investigation have modified
this view, and to-day Zoroaster is recog
nized as a distinct historic personality.
We even know something of his lif*. \V y e
know how, like other reformers, he be
came dissatisfied with the grossness of the
national faith and for years lived apart in
search of the truth. We know that he be
gan his mission of preaching at the age of
30, and we know the name of the Prince
(Vishtaspa) who finally accepted his doc
trine and became for Iran what Constan
tine was for Christendom. We even know
that the prophet was thrice married
(strange as this may seem) and that he
left children.
The doctrine of Zoroaster Is contained In
the so-called Avesta, the only relic of the
Avestan language, which, as we have
seen, was closely akin to ancient Per3!an.
The Avesta is a collection of rel'glous
laws, myths, litanies and hymns, now eas
ily accessible in translation, and notwith
standing an occasional note of true spirit
uality must be regarded on the whole'as
one of the dreariest and least interesting
ii ".he world's Bibles. It requires an ex
traordinary- effort of Impgina'ion to under
stand how so much childish folly and rw
true religious spirit could be conr>blned.
Essentially the faith of Zoroaster Is a pro
nounced dualism. On the one side stood
Ormazd and the angels of light; on the
other side are ranged Ahriman and the
princes of darkness. Into this ceaseless
struggle man is thrown and must bear his
part in the conflict. His duty is to pre
serve his purity intact; uncleanness Is the
great sin. So It was that death was looked
upon as uncleanness, and the most char
acteristic rite was then, and is still among
the Pariseps, the custom of exposing dead
bodies to be devoured by the fowls of th 3
air, so that neither earth nor water nor
fire should be made Impure by decay. To
us the most Interesting fact In connection
with Zoroastrianlsm Is Its Infiltration lntÂ»
Christianity through Manichaeism and it 3
