The Burns Handicap Winner
Ran Unplaced in a Race
at Oakland.
>
Sea Bass, Favorite for the Two-Year-
Old Scramble, Injured at the
Post â Potente a Mud
Runner.
Imperious, the fortunate winner of the
great Burns handicap, started in a seven
furlong free handicap at Oakland yester
day, finishing outside of the money, being
beaten by Potente, Pompino and Good
Hope. He closed equal favorite In the
betting with Potente and never looked
dangerous. The latter horse picked up 122
pounds, winning in a gallop by three
lengths from Pompino, in 1:28, a remark
ably tine performance, and It is to be re
gretted the Racine gelding did not start in
last Saturday's big race.
Although the track was very muddy and
rain fell at intervals,- four favorites
splashed in ahead of their fields.
A said fate befell Sea Bass, favorite for
the. two-year-old scramble. The half
sister to Recreation was bumped into and
injured in the shoulder during a false
breakaway, the accident compelling her
to be left at the post. The winner turned
up in Mamie Hildreth, a 15 to 1 shot,
which downed Irate and Mrs. Brunell
very cleverly the last sixteenth.
The erratic San Tomas started in the
opening five and a half furlong scramble
In the name of Fred Edwards, opening a
2 to 1 favorite over the field. His price
went to 5, when It was swiped to 16 to 5,
Mountebank enjoying the honors of favor
tlsm. Buchanan got San Tomas away
well and the colt proved a wonder In the
mud, running like a ghost. He tried to
bolt in the stretch, just scaling the outside
fence, and then won easily from the fa
vorite. Morella, against which 25 to 1
to show was laid, ran third.
Barney Schreiber's fast marÂ© Miss Mar
ion made her reappearance In the sprint
which followed, over the Futurity course,
ruling a pronounced choice in the ring.
Leading almost from the start, she was
hard ridden at the end to beat the 20 to 1
outsider El Mido.
It did not look very rosy for the back
ers of Essence during the early stages of
the seven furlong run. The Burns & Wat
erhouse entry could not keep up, but stood
a lot of riding, and ridden out earned the
verdict from Tekla by a couple of lengths.
Matt Hogan, after chasing out in front
with Choteau, died away to nothing.
Eddie Ross landed the mile and a fur
long run with Malay. Dr. Rowell's horse
had not shown much form of late, but his
well-known liking for mud made him an
equal favorite with Tappan. Morse,
astride the 20 to 1 outsider Uarda, came
dangerously close to stealing a march on
the field. Getting away in the lead .it
took the hardest sort of riding on the
part of Ross to overtake and beat her a
neck on the wire. Castake ran third."

RATE FOR LIGHTS
WILL BE MODIFIED
Supervisors' Committee and Mayor
Phelan Decide on a Maximum
Flat Bate With No :
Discount.
The Supervisors' Artificial Lights Com
mittee met yesterday In conference with
Mayor Phelan and the representatives of
several light companies for the purpose of
considering certain modifications in the
electric light rates adopted as a result of
the recent investigation. It was decided
to fix a maximum flat rate for electric arc
lights, instead of the high rate subject to
discount as heretofore adopted, and to
permit the discount to apply only to In
candescent lights. No definite conclusion
was arrived at, though the commute*
rather favored an Increase from $1 75 to
$1 95 on electric lamps" lit until midnight.
President Crockett of the San Francisco
Company made a plea that the rate of 11
cents for Incandescent lights In the out
side districts be raised to 15 cents, which,
he claimed, was the maximum rate In
other large cities. Manager E. J. Martin
of the Harbor and Western Power Com
pany said that the $175 rate for electric
lamps until 12 o'clock In the downtown
districts was a competitive one; that in
Oakland, where there was no competition,
the rate Is $2 To,' and in Stockton 52 50. He
argued that the 12 o'clock light cost more
than $1 75, and that the rate should be si
fixed as to comprehend every system ex
cept the direct Edison system. The mat
ter of .depreciation, he said,, had not been
taken into account; nor the loss In thÂ« dis
tribution of current. in the outlying dU
trlcts. '
A. M. Hunt of the Independent Light
and Power Company stated that if the
San Francisco Company had proper trans
formers it would not sustain bo great *
loss in distribution, and that If the 11-cent
rate was adopted it would force the com
pany to secure. a better system to reduce
the waste. to a minimum.
W." G. Summerhayes of the Mutual *7om "
pany s said , that his Â« company charged , In
some cases' sl 50 for 12 o'clock lights, and
made money at that rate. -Â¦ â Â¦ .
: The committee will meet again for the
purpose of arranging a proper modification
of the rates, which will be resubmitted at
next Monday's meeting of the board la
the form of an amendment.

ATE AND ATE AND
YARNED AND YARNED
Fly-Casters Swap Many New Tales
Over the Banquet Board During
Their Bimonthly Dinner.
About fifty members of the San Fran
cisco Fly Casting Club met at dinner last
night In a downtown rotlsserte and told
fish stories and discussed an elaborate
repast. It was the bimonthly banquet of
the well-known organization and the
downpour and other attractions did not
keep the members from attending.
An excellent orchestra furnished popu
lar music during the evening and the men
who search the streams for festive- trout
and other, speckled beauties raised their
voices in song. | Between courses the Jolly
devotees of Izaak Walton swapped fish
stories, and although Home of the tales
told would have made Munchausen look
like a selling-plater the narrator's verac
ity was never questioned as his neighbor
at the table had one to tell, that was
equally strong. Attorney Alexander T.
Vogelsang tried to make C. H. Young ba
lieve that he had enjoyed great fishing in
the Dead Sea; "Doc" Watt impressed on
his auditors that in years past he won
many trophies in drop-line tournaments;
President W. D. Mansfield said he thought
he could cast a fly over the Spreckels
building If Â¦ the wind favored htm, and
other members were prepared with simi
lar assertions. The vÃostonian Quartet,
composed of Messrs. Kelly, Wendell,
Kllnk.'Fraser and Manager Lambert, was
heard in tuneful selections. Seated at the
banquet board were:
E. T. Allen, W. F. Bogart, H. Battu.

Cooper, A. R. Crowell, J. H. De Witt.

Rev. B. Fay Mills to Lecture.
A meeting of representative citizens was
held yesterday afternoon in the offices of
Dr. S. F. Long, 135 Geary street, for the
purpose of forming an association which
will be known as the Sunday Lecture As
sociation. The object of the association
is to secure the continuous services of
Dr B. Fay Mills for a course of lectures
to be delivered In Metropolitan Temple
every Sunday afternoon. The lectures,
while of a religious nature, will be strictly
non-sectarian in character. The first
lecture. It Is expected, will be delivered
next Sunday afternoon. The following of
ficers have been elected to preside over
the affairs of the society: President,
Judge James G. Maguire: first vice presi
dent James T. Rodgers; second vice pres
ident, Mrs. M. F. Gray; secretary, Rev.
George B. Allen.

FOR ABSOLUTE DIVORCE
His Wife Says He Abandoned Her in
Oregon â Decrees Granted and
Suits Filed.
Rev. C. M. Lane, at one time pastor of
an Episcopal church In the Mission, ha 3
been sued for divorce by his wife,' Lulu
Lane, for desertion and failure to pro
vide. Mrs. Lane, who resides at 213 Valen
cia street, alleges that her husband de
serted her at Albany. Or., January 11. 1599.
Mrs. Lane states that she married her
husband at Sacramento on January 30,
1895; that he mistreated her from that
date and finally abandoned her.
Decrees of divorce have been granted
Carrie H. "Whlttier from James E. Whit
tier on the ground of habitual Intemper
ance and Albert J. Geromai from Annie
N. Geromai on the ground of desertion.
Catherine Conroy has sued Daniel A.
Conroy for a divorce, alleging desertion
as cause of action.

NATIVE SONS' HALL - - - - 414 MASON STREET
Influence in this way on so many of thÂ«
European heresies or the middle ages.
.The great and merited fame of Fitzger
ald s Rubaiyat has made one of the Per
sian poets a true English classic, and
though this version of Omar is so free as
to be a paraphrase rather than a transla
tion, yet the voluptuous and semt-myttl
cal beauty of Fitzgerald's lines la not a
bad representation of the Persian lyrics.
It would be interesting to study at larga
the other famous lyric poets â H&flz. At
tar, Saadi and the restâ but to-day we
must concentrate our attention rather
upon the great epic writer whose work
precedes and outshines them all.
We have seen how Persian literature Is
divided Into three distinct periods, and
how the third or modern period begins
about the year 1000 A. D. with the work
of Firdusl. L*t us look for a moment at
the life of this famous author. Flrdust
was born In a small village near Tus. In
the year WO of our era. Already a poet
of the name of Dekiki had undertaken
to weave the old Persian traditions into
a continuous narrative, but had left hi 3
work unfinished. .Inspired by this exam
ple, the young Flrdusi set himself to re
cover the ancient legends of his land.
Much he found written In books, much
he took down from the oral traditions
among the people, for the people of Iran
had not forgotten the glorious deeds of
their ancestors, and, indeed, to this day
these traditions are handed down by
memory from father to son. This great
body of legends the poet wrought into a
long narrative of some 80,000 couplets.
Having got this vast work well under
way, he set out for the court. There is
a curious story of an adventure on the
road. In a garden near the capital he
happened to come upon three court poeta
drinking wine and amusing themselves
with extemporizing verse. Thinking to
have sport with the stranger, they asked
him to cap a stanza for them. Choosing
a most difficult rhyme, they each pro
nounced a line, whereupon, to their
amazement, the newcomer added a fourth,
which contained a historical allusion quite
unknown to them. As a reward, they in
troduced him to the court, where Flrdusl
soon won the favor of Shah Mahmud.
Never had such verse as his been heard,
and all at first were glad to do him honor.
In the end, however. Jealousy and malice
found him. He was accused of slighting
the Mohammedan faith for the ancient
religion of his fathers; he was cheated
of his promised payment, and. In disgust,
having added a few lines of bitter satire
against the shah at tbe end of his com
pleted poem, he fled from the court and
hid himself in his native town. Here he
died a poor man, but tradition declares
that the shah repented at last and that
the messenger bearing his bounty entered
the gate of the town at the very mo
ment when the poet's body was being car
ried out to his last resting place.
The epic of Flrdusi. "The Shah Na
meh, or Book of Kings." Is well described
by its name. It Is a continuous narrative
of Persian history from Gayumers, the
fabulous first man and king of the race,
down to the invasion of the Arabs In the
seventh century. A great number of epi
sodes are woven Into the thread, and In
deed the poem Is bewildering by Its very
richness of material. In plan It is thus
quite different from the epics of Greece
and India and Germany, each of which
is made to center about one historic event.
Yet If the "Shah Nameh" lacks the unity
of the "Iliad" or the "Ramayana," It haa
another kind of unity of Its own.
We have seen that the old religion of
Iran was strongly duallstlc. and this dual-
Ism Is the central and harmonizing motive
of Flrdusl's poem. Here again we see the
everlasting conflict of light and darkness,
of truth and falsehood, waging on the
earth. History and mythology have been
inextricably confused. The old mythi
cal powers of light are now the Persian
people themselves, while the princes of
darkness are the aborigines and Tura
nians with whom Iran was continually at
war. This religious background lends a
certain moral earnestness to the poem,
and is one of Its most pleasing features.
Flrdusi has changed the fatth of his fath
ers Into history and woven it Into the very
texture of his plot: the doctrine of Mo
hammed, of which he was nominally a
follower, he has for the most part avoid
ed, saving himself by giving a certain
deistlc tone to the work. At bottom fate Is
the power that Flrdusl really worships â
fate that mocks human affairs and raises
up the mighty only in the end to toss them,
into the dust. At the close of each epoch
of events he Is wont to add some such sad
moral as thiss "Look ye! "Who could at
tain a glory like to this man'a? He hath
shown to men how riches are won, but h*
hath not enjoyed riches. â¢ The world Is
but a passing dream: neither happiness
nor sorrow endures." And again: "O
world, cease to raise us up only to gather
us as a harvest at thÂ© end! If thou wish
est that we vanish away, why, then, raise
us up? Thou exaltest a man above the
firmament, and straightway hnrlest him
under the obscure earth."

TRIO OF ACCIDENTS
CAUSED BY WAGONS
A Woman and a Man Knocked Down
and Injured and a Teamster Has
His Skull Fractured.
Mrs. Charles Paulson, who lives at Point
Bonita lighthouse, Marin County, was
crossing Market street at Sixth yesterday
afternoon when she . was struck ani
knocked down by a delivery wagon be
longing to Jelllch & Roolick, provision
dealers, 213 Sixth street. She was picked
up and carried to a drugstore near by,
where It was found that she was badly
Injured. The driver will be arrested.
Harry Kenlo, 489 Bryant street, was
crossing Third and Harrison streets when
he was knocked down by an express
wagon driven by Lem Sing, a Chinese. He
was taken to the Receiving Hospital to
have cuts and bruises on his body and
face attended to. Lem Sing was arrested
on a charge of battery. \
Arthur J. Walker, a teamster for Gray
Brothers, was lirlvlng a load of sand along
Market street, and at Eighth street he tell
from his seat to the ground. He wag
picked up in an unconscious condition and
taken to the Receiving Hospital. It is
supposed that his skull was fractured. He
Is an old pensioner.
MORE BAD LUCK
FOR HANCOCK'S
STEAM LAUNCH
Turned With the Tide and
Went on the Blades of
the Ship's Screw.
?
Italian Cruiser Calabria Has Been
Ordered to Japan â Insurance In
creased on Annie Thomas.
Changes of Captains.
The new launch built by the Union Iron
Works for the United States transport
Hancock is playing In hard luck. The
fates seem to be against the Email craft,
and it will be many a day, if ever, before
she again plies her calling on the bay of
San Francisco. Monday night she got foul
of the Hancock's screw, and the "loles
that were punched in her side by the
blades caused her to fill and sink.
A watchman was asleep in the launch,
and as soon as he discovered the mishap
he called for assistance. The launch waa
got around to the ship's davits and an at
tempt was made to get her aboard. The
weight was too much for the hoisting
gear, however, and as the launch broke
away she almost tore the davits out of
the ship.' The men on deck threw a rope
to the watchman and he managed to clanj
ber up the Hancock's side. In a few m'n
utes the launch went down stern first, *nd
although a half-dozen boats were out
grappling for her yesterday no trace of
her could be found.
"When the Hancock was being over
hauled and made into a model transput
the new launch was added to her outfit.
Like the Honcock. she was, the finest
thing of her kind afloat, and all the other
transports envied Captain Struve his fine
launch. A few days ago while lying at the
Folaom street float one of the tubes in
the launch's boiler blew out and several
men were scalded. The necessary repairs
were made and the launch was again in
service, but only for a couple of days, and
then came this laat mishap. After th-s
day's work was done the launch was made
fast to the transport and allowed to drift
astern. When the tide turned and the
ship began to swing the boat came around
under the transport's stern and in a very
short space of time she stove holes in her
self on the propeller blades and went
down.
The destination of the Italian man-of
war Calabria has been changed. Ins tend
of coming to San Francisco she is going
to Japan and from there to China. The
Italian Consul received a telegraphic order
yesterday to forward all mail for the
cruiser to Yokohama. Expected trouble
In the Orient over the Chinese question
has called the Calabria to Japan, so tho
Italian colony will not get Its expected
treat.
The British ship Annie Thomas is now
out 221 days from Cardiff for Acapulco.
and yesterday 70 per c*Â»nt was offered to
reinsure the vessel and cargo. The only
thing against the risk is the fact that the
ship has a coal cargo. If she has not been
afire then there is no reason why tlii
Thomas should rot show up all right. Ehe
is a notoriously slow ship, and again
everything is against her making time in
the Gulf of Panama. The schooner Sophie
Sutherland, owned here, is now out seventy
days from El Triumfo for this port, but
no anxiety is felt over her non-arrival;
fo shipping men reason that the 70 per
cent on the Annie Thomas is a good gam
ble.
Captain A. I. Lowell, who resigned from
the lighthouse service some months ago,
will take command of Timothy Hopkins'
yacht El Primero. Captain Morehor.se
has taken command of the bark Diamond
Head, vice Captain Ward, who will go
East to bring out one of the new vessels
of the Wllliams-Dlmond fleet. Captain
Davis, formerly of the ship Tacoma, has
gone to Puget Sound to take command of
the May Flint, and Captain Hanson had
taken command of the steamer Noyo.

New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, March t.â Conditions were
mixed end the movement of prices was very
much confused on the Stock Exchange to-day.
Fentlment Is correspondingly conflicting among
the speculative contingent. The situation may
be beet described as one of waiting on the
part of the believers in higher prices. Those
having faith in a decline took advantage of
the hesitation of the bulls and the entire lack
of outside interest In the stock market to
make vigorous attacks at vulnerable spots in
the market. The results on the whole were
favorable to the bears. They were very dili
gent in tetklng out stocks which were unpro
tected by supporting orders and in offering
them down belcw the price level where selling
orders were known to be placed in the stocks
for the purpose of having 1<jÂ»Â»Â«s on any sud
cen decline. ThÂ» sudden fall In prices of stocks
thus selected and equally sudden and violent
rallies gave the key to the character of this
selling. Tlie flurry in the rat* for call money
.In the late afternoon to 4 per cent was a
farortna; factor for the bears, but the actual
effect was very small. The early tendency to
!adrance in the railroad list was checked by
the maneuvers cf the bears and tb* more
notable advances were about wiped out. The
railroad list yielded unwillingly and only to a
Flight degree In spite of the light demand and
a sprinkling of gains was retained at the
close. Very earnest efforts were made to
weaken Sngar, but the utmost that could be
achieved was a three-point decline, offerings
cf tb* stock being comparatively light. Third
Avenue was strong, rising an extreme C% and
Imparting tyrspathetla strength to the other
local traction stocks. The most notable ex
amplee of the bear tactics were furnished by
the. international paper stocks, the common
falling 4% and rallying :%. and the preferred
falling 7% and rallying 4; Pressed Steel Car.
which dropped Â«% and rallied 6: American
Hoop, which fell 2 and rallied 1%. and Standard
KopÂ« end Its bold issue, which were raided
down from 2% to S points, with rallies ex
tending from 1 to nearly 4 points, and People's
O*Â«. which fell 1% and rallied 2%. Practically
all the iron and eteel stocks were down from
1 to 2 points and many recovered a good por
tion of the loss and there were other less
striking examples all through the list of spec
ialties. In the railroad list St. Paul, Burling
tun ar.d Baltimore and Ohio showed an ex
treme decline of a point or over, but the net
changes In the railroads are small throughout.'
It 1a evident that the final disposition of the
financial bill by Congress is awaited by the
bull contingent, who are generally confident
that the provisions for additional bank circu
lation will be enacted into law and will re-
FUlt in a notable expansion of the currency.
The large buying of Government bonds by na
if nal banks all ov<>r the country and the ex
j^-rt estimates which ar* current of the profit
<;fi>r*<i on circulation by the new provisions
pre the grounds of this oonfldenc*. Meantime
the money market is working constantly closer
md the favorable factors In the outlook are
ignored In the fear that the available supply
of money will not bridge the interval until the
expected relief.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL

The pressure haE fallen steadily over the
northern half of the country. A moderate
degression over Vancouver Island and North
ern Washington ha* caused a general rain on
the coa*t north of San Francisco. The storm
will probably move eastward throuEh Idaho
and Montana. The weather is generally cloudy
peer the Pacific slop* north of the Tehachapi.
No rain has fallen in Southern California and

LONDON. March Â«.â The second series of the
1900 wool auction sales opened to-day with a
large attendance of buyers. Small and fairly
representative catalogues were offered. Merinos
and crossbreds showed a decline of 7% to 10
per cent. The Continental buyers operated
chiefly in the former grades and the home trade
purchased crossbreds. '
Competition generally was fairly active,
especially for lambs, which showed a decline
of 5 per cent. The offerings to-day numbered
7463 bales.

CHICAGO, March 6.â When the day opened
In the wheat pit there was a bare prospect â
nothing of Influence either wayâ and accord
ingly May wheat opened unchanged from yes
terday at Cj%c. So dull wag the situation thatÂ»
the attempt of a discouraged long to dispose
of 250,000 bushels of wheat, which went in the
course of an hour through a score of small
hands, sent the price of May to 65% c. At this
point the signal service prediction of a cold
wave and the Liverpool advance in spot wheat
came as a tonic which Imparted firmness and
moderate activity. Shorts, led by St. Louis in
terests, covered and May rallied to 66c. On
prcnt-taUtng which met the bulge 6Â»%@65%c
was touched, but the market rebounded, re
ceiving further help from a report of a good
Inquiry at New York, the firmness of the
Northwest and a pessimistic view of crop con
ditions In some sections. May rallied to 66% c
and closed strong %Â©VÂ£c over yesterday at ;
66<tf'66Hc. . j
The corn market was strong and moderately
active. Country offerings were very small and
receipts light and apparently the recent at
tempt to break the market had been aban- \
doned, for the gain shown in opening figures
found few sellers and shorts were disposed |to :
cover. May closed at the top, %c over yes
terday. -
The strength of corn lent enough of Its In
fluence to the neglected oats market to In
cline that very dull cereal toward firmness.
May closed %c over yesterday at . 23V4c.
The provision trade experienced the dullest
day In a very dull fortnight, but the declining i
tendency due to a weak hog market and a
poor demand was . checked by the strength of
corn and the dose was steady. May pork
closed Be over yesterday. May lard 2%c up
and May ribs 2%c higher.

Hathor .... EnropÂ« Mar. U.Mechanics' Institute Affairs.
The trustees of the Mechanics' Institute
met last night to make arrangements for
the annual meeting of the members to
be held next Saturday night, when the
seven new trustees elected a week ago
will be Installed. The following commit
tee was appointed to conduct the installa
tion ceremony: A. S. Hallidie, Irving M.
fccott and Henry L. Davis.
The members will vote on two Impor
tant amendments to the by-laws of the
Institute. The first provides that no trus
tee shall be eligible, for any salaried, posi
tion until the expiration of the term for
which he was elected shall have expired.
The second amendment is intended to sub
stitute the Australian ballot for the pres
ent preferential system of voting. At the
conclusion of the members' meeting the
newly elected and holdover trustees will
meet and organize by selecting officers to
serve for the ensuing year. Considerable
speculation attaches to whether -Cojonel
E. A. Dentcke will be chosen to succeed
himself as president.

FIGHT CONVICT-MADE GOODS.
Western Manufacturers Hope for Be
lief FTom an Unfair Competition.
A movement Is on foot headed by tha
Manufacturers' and Producers* Associa
tion to revive the crusade against the ship
ping into this State goods made by con
victs in prisons of other States of the
Union. When this body was organized the
matter of convict-made goods was about
the first to be investigated, and a vast
amount of data was gathered showing
that the State of California was and Â«3
the dumping ground of the products of all
the prisons in the country. Efforts were
made then to have the national authori
ties pass laws by which the West would
be protected from convict competition,
against 'free labor, but the effort failed.
The matter was not allowed to rest, for
as soon as Congressman Julius Kahn
went to Washington he started the ball
rolling with another bill which the man
ufacturers of the West appreciated, and
urged the other Pacific Coast representa
tives In Congress to support. The effort
does not end here, for the association has
decided to call upon all of the commer
cial Industrial and labor union bodies of
the State to rally to Its support.

north as does the Haywards line to the
south. Before the Haywards line was built
there was nothing but unbroken fields to
San Leandro. . Now the country is thickly
settled. The. same thing can t>e done to
the north, and the road will make a pa
tronage for Itself that will be legitimate
and profitable.
"The road will be built just as soon as
we get the franchises. We asked for that
in Contra Costa County, first because we
expected that it would take longer to se
cure that portion. We will ask for those'
in Berkeley, Emeryville and Oakland just
as soon as we are assured of getting that
in Contra Costa County. We will probably
locate the power-house In Oakland. [ As
for the line into Oakland, we will have to
take some other streets after we reach
West Berkeley, but there are many rootea
that can be. secured. The total length of
the line will be about fifteen miles, and it
will coat about $6000 a mile to build.
"Of course, the route of our line shows
that we will go to Point Richmond, but
the Santa Fe system Is not behind our
line, and neither is the Southern Pacific.
We may get some patronage from that
source, but our line will be independent. 1
OAKLAND, March 6.â The applica
tion of William G. Henshaw and
A. S. Macdonald of this city for a
franchise for an* electric railroad
between Point Richmond and San
Pablo and Oakland, as was told exclusive
ly In The Call this morning, means much
to Oakland. It means a definite connec
tion between the present terminus of the
Santa Fe Railroad system at Point Rich
mond and the city of Oakland. It means
a, solution of the question that has been
so often and so seriously raised in this
city, "How will the Santa Fe reach Oak
land?" It certainly will furnish a pas
senger connection between Point Rich
mond and Oakland for the Santa Fe sys
tem and can be made to furnish a freight
connection should the road bo wish.
.So the Importance of. the move Is ob
vious to Oakland. No matter whether an
Independent line, built and controlled by
local capitalists or whether t directly
backed by the Santa Fe Railroad system,
It will be the connecting link between
Oakland and Point Richmond for that
road for a long while.
A. S. Macdonald, one of those in whose
name the franchise was asked, finally said
to-day that the road was an independent
affair, backed by neither the Santa Fe
nor the Southern Pacific systems. But
while Mr. Macdonald would not admit
anything but Independent support for his
proposed. road he did admit that it would
be for a while, at least, a connection be
tween . the Santa Fe terminus at Point
Richmond and Oakland.
An Inspection of the map that shows the
route of the proposed electric railroad will
make It rather difficult for people to be
lieve that it has not some very close con
nection with the Santa Fe system. The
most important part of the line runs in a
loop around the big peninsula known as
the potrero of San Pablo, of which Point
Richmond Is a projection into deep water.
i But not only does ..the proposed electric
line make a loop around the potrero, but
!it runs directly down Point Richmond
itself, to . the very ferry terminus of the
Santa Fe system. As Point Richmond and
the land for some little distance back Is
owned by the Santa Fe Railroad It Is
quite evident that It will be necessary to
secure the consent of the big railroad be
fore the electric railroad can construct Its
most important line.
It is so evident that the proposed elec
tric road will be constructed for the pur
pose principally of reaching Point Rich
mond and the Santa Fe terminus and that
It will draw its main source of revenue
from its connection with- that- terminus,
that no matter what the backing of the
road it becomes the Santa Fe's connection
with Oakland. -
This Is all that the people of Oakland
want to know and this The Call told them
before any other paper this morning.
The money necessary to construct the
line has all been secured, but the two
gentlemen who have asked for the fran
chise are amply able to raise the money
themselves without railroad or Eastern
backing. -
This Is the first appearance of both Mr.
Henshaw and Mr. Mncdonald as railroad
proprietors. Mr. Macdonald made his de- â¢
but as a railroad promoter by deftly and
successfully securing the rights of way
for the Santa Fe road f rom Â¦ Antloch to
Point Richmond, a piece of work which
he did to the satisfaction of the road. Mr.
Henshaw has long been Interested in
many enterprises In Oakland, but never hi
a railroad. A. S. Macdonald to-day made
a more detailed statement abqut his pro
posed railroad, saying:
"This will be an Independent line, and
It will be built just as soon as the fran
chises are granted. The road will be built
by the ones in v.-hose name the franchise
was asked, "William G. Henshaw an-J my
self. We believe there Is a legitimate lieM
for such a road, built and operated on thÂ«
same plan as the Oakland, San LeanUro
and Hay wards electric line. It- will not
be quite so long a line as the Haywards
MARRIED LIFE
WAS TOO MUCH
FOR BROMWELL
Happy Social Event of Two
Seasons Ago Ends in the
Divorce Court.
Claire Belle Bromwell Given a Decree
of Separation Because Her Hua
â¢ band Failed- to Provide
.. :>:â¢ for Her.' U~%
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
111S Broadway, March 6.
The marriage of Claire Belle Haswell
and Percy Bromwell. celebrated with
great eclat Just two years ago yesterday,
ended this afternoon, when - Superior
Judge Hall granted Mrs. Bromwell a de
cree of divorce on the ground of failure
to provide.
The trial of the divorce suit was ons
of the shortest on record. The only wit
nesses were Mrs. Bromwell. her mother
and County Assessor Dalton. Attorney
George de Golia. on behalf of the defend
ant, offered no testimony. It appeared
that young Mr. Bromwell took his bride
to the home of his father. Colonel Brom
well. to reside. They lived together hap
pily for a while, but the burdens of mat
rimonial life soon became unbearable to
the young groom. For some reason he
left his position as a deputy under County
Assessor Dalton. In which. he earned {100
a month, and since last June he has ab
solutely failed to contribute anything to
ward the support of his wife. No ali
mony was asked for.
Mrs. Bromwell is a prominent member
of the First M. E. Church choir.

CAPTAIN BUFORD MUST
PAY FOR BAKER'S BREAK
Master of the Grant Haled Before the
Collector , for Preventing - Cus
toms Officials Prom Board
ing the Grant.
Tho arbitrary action of officers of the
Government transport .Grant In refusing
to allow customs inspectors to board the
transport on her arrival last Monday has
been reported by Customs Surveyor Spear
to Port Collector Jackson and, In accord
ance with the report, charges were filed
with the Collector yesterday. â¢ ?
The navigation laws or the United
States hold the captain responsible for the
sailing of the vessel, â¢ hence the charges
were made against Captain Buford of the
Grant, and he will be offered up as a vi
carious sacrifice for the sins of Quarter
master Baker. The penalty Is a fine of
not less than $50 nor more than $500.

FOUND BURGLARS AT HOME.
Housebreakers Appear on Stockton
and on Buchanan Streets.
When Joseph Watson, an employe In the
Mint, and his wife returned to their home,
ISSO Stockton street, late Monday night,
after visiting a friend,' they were .sur
prised to see every light in the house
burning. Suspecting that burglars were
in the house, Watson made a rush for
his revolver, but before he could procure
It he saw a man climbing out of a rear
window.
Watson sounded an alarm, but the
burglar made his escape. Every room
in the house had been thoroughly, search
ed by the burglar, but the only things
missing were two pairs of trousers and
about J3 In coin. Mrs. Watson fortunately
had her jewelry with her. The burglary
was reported at police headquarters yes
terday.
Another burglar pot Into the residence
of W. H. Gage, 201 Buchanan stret, Mon
day night and carried off several articles
of clothing and jewelry. The police were
notified yesterday.
POSTTJM CEKEAJL.
UNNATURAL HUNGER
A Sure Sign of Hidden Dyspepsia.
"It was necessary to eat dinner at 11
o'clock" (says Mrs. C F. Oilman, 1131
Preston St.. Rockford, 111.), "in order to
have strength to prepare the noonday
mealfor the family.
"While I was drinking coffee I felt so
faint at n o'clock that I was unable to
proceed with the work unless I had
lunch. If I missed the 11 o'clock meal
I was attacked with a severe sick head-
ache.
"My complexion at that time was a
sight, great blotches appearine on my
face, and I was so* nervous I could
sleep but a few minutes at a time, and
would wake in the morning more tired
than when I went to bed.
"Our grocer called my attention one
day to Postum Cereal Coffee. This was
about three years ago. I immediately
quit the use of coffee and took up Pos-
ttim, having it prepared properly. The
change produced a remarkable result.
In a week or two I was. able to leave off
the H o'clock lunch and take my dinner
in the regular way with the rest of the
family. My blotchy complexion disap-
peared and a natural complexion took
its place. Now I can go from morning
until night without a meal, if I desire,
and no headache or inconvenience of
any kind appears. I sleep sound as a
babyÂ» and my kidney trouble, which was
more than serious, has entirely disap-
peared. 'i'*j' ;:
"A lady friend was recommended to
try Postum, and a short time after told
me fhe was disgusted with it for it had
no ta?tc. I asked her, if she boiled it
carefully fifteen minutes after the real
bubbling commenced. Shesaid no. and
in reply to another question said she
used only one heaping teaspoonful to
the cup. I explained to her that she
must use two heaping teaspoons to the
cup and let it boil long enough. The
next time I saw her she said she used
Postum regularly and \ liked it very

ANIMALS SHOULD BE
TREATED WITH KINDNESS
Professor Norris Gives Some Sound
Advice to Amateur Dog Pan
. ciers â How to Train a Â¦
Canine.
Professor Norris of Norrls & Rowe's big
trained animal shows, which Is crowding
the Mechanics' Pavilion every afternoon
and evening, has some sound advice to
give owners of dumb beast pets. He has
handled hundreds of animals In his career
and speaks from experience when he
states that the only way to teach them
tricks is by kindness and patience.
"Of all the animals that inhabit the
earth there are few, if any, subjected to
the variety of treatment visited upon the
dog," said Professor Norris. "Probably
the horse's is the nearest approach to the
dog's life, but the simile Is hardly paral
lel, owing to the matter of dollars and
cents and value, which occasionally stay 3
the hand of a cruel owner for fear that
the result of an exhibition of temper
would make sad Inroads on the pocketr
book in case the animal had to be re
placed. While in many instances the
values of dogs have reached almost fab
ulous sums owing to superior breeding,
bench show or field dualities, yet after
many years of practically educating the
public by exhibition or otherwise, people
exist who only know a dog as simply a
common animal with four legs, which
barks, occasionally bites, and is a pet or
a nuisance as the case may be. The field
of usefulness occupied by the dog Is very
extensive. Its scope depending much on
the variety, its education and subsequent
treatment. â¢ : â¢
Happily, of late years the advancement
of civilization has developed a strong sen
timent in favor of the merciful treatment
of dumb animals and condemning unnec
essary harshness or punishment, its gen
eral effect being a more useful treatment
of dogs and a disposition on the part of
the owner or handler to give man's best
friend opportunities hitherto denied him.
The puppy should be educated in a firm
yet quiet way, the trainer taking pains
to Impress upon the pupil the value of
obedience as a cardinal virtue in the ca
nine reportolre. -While some people take
a fiendish delight in teasing a dog, It ii
not to be expected that they realize what
they are doing, or a different course would
be pursued. A dog should be educated to
become 1 accustomed to the chain only for
purposes of temporary restraint; it bofnÂ«
understood that on practical occasions
perfect freedom Is conducive to good
health and amiable disposition, perma
nent restraint generally resulting In sour
dispositions, which could easily be avoided
if proper methods were employed.

SCORES OF CONVERTS
MADE BY BOY PREACHER
Master Jack Cook Doing Phenomenal
Work at the Howard-Street
. , ,:. M. E. Church.
Master Jack Cook, the boy evangelist,
who is conducting revival services at
Howard-street M. E. Church, is nightly
crowding the edifice and winning many
converts to his cause. Despite the rain
last night a large crowd waited in front
of the church as early as 6 o'clock to gain
admittance. The services lasted -intl!
nearly midnight, and the boy preacher,
aided by his father, Henry Cook, and his
brother, Hayden Cook, aroused great re
ligious fervor among the audience.
Rev. John A. B. Wilson opened last
night's service with prayer, and at its
conclusion -young Cook devoted half .an
hour to answering complex questions on
the philosophy of the Bible. He chose hls>
text from a suggestion given him by one
of those present, and then delivered an
eloquent sermon, in which he appealed
for converts. He succeeded In inducing
twenty-one converts to. step before the
altar and he then led them in prayer.
The young preacher's services are great
ly in, demand. Committees from Fresno,
Sacramento and Woodland have appealed
to him to come to their respective towns
and lead revival services." Master Cook
will probably remain at the Howard-street
M. E. Church, for. another week. ;' *-"

boy, thirty-eight years ago, until now.
Moreover, he authorizes the Senator to
present his (Corbln's) resignation. to tha
President if the search reveals a sugges
tion of unworthiness. The letter Includes
the findings of the court-martial which
honorably acquitted Lieutenant Colonel
Corbln of the charge, General Grant's let
ter conferring upon him two brevets for
gallant services at the same time he was
charged with cowardice, and, finally, a
letter from the colonel of the regiment
who preferred the charge, praising his
work in the .campaign.

GADSKI AND BISPHAM
IN CHARMING CONCERT
Mr. Damrosch Analyzes and Illus
trates Wagner's "Tannhauser,"
Assisted by the Two
Great Stars.
Although Mr. Walter Damroseh's prelim
inary recital prefaced the present series
of "Wagnerian contorts most . agreeably,
public Interest centered In the reappear
ance of Madame Gadski, which took place
at the California Theater yesterday af
ternoon. In point of artistic Importance,
however, our first hearing of David Bis
pham must take priority.
The cpncert was devoted to an exposi
tion of "Tannhauser," and it was an
agreeable Â¦ surprise to many to find that
Mr. Damro^h again contributed a verbal
analysis ana elucidation of the composer's
art and purpose. This introduction of fa
miliar talks by Mr. Damrosch adds much
to the pleamre that is to be derived from
listening to his admirable illustrations on
the piano and to the singing of Madame
Gadski, Mr. Bispham and Mr. Schott.
The conduct of the concerts Is as inter
esting as it is novel, and the combination
of discourse and vocal illustration is a
substitute for complete operatic treatment
more satisfying than one could anticipate.
Mr. Damrosch compasses on the piano a
degree of orchestral effect that is posi
tively astounding. His execution is dis
tinguished as much by delicacy and a feel
ing for the spiritual as it is by breadth
and vigor. He is not less happy In inter
preting Wagner's exquisite treble sugges
tions of the supernatural and abstract
than-ln the forceful rendering of his stur
dier dramatic themes. ; He alternately
narrates,' declaims, muses, storms, pleads,
defies, laments. In short, runs the gamut
of dramatic and emotional expression.
"Tannhauser" is lacking In point of
technical interest as compared with the
later Wagnerian music-dramas, of which
the complex thematic treatment Is sus
ceptible of more copious analysis; but Mr.
Damrosch traced the thread of the story
and its musical eetting most delightfully,
and again won the auditors by his sincer
ity and charm of manner.
Madame Gadski was graciously wel
comed and sang her portions of the opera
with admirable art. Once regarded only
as a lyric soprano, she has essayed the
heavier dramatic roles with such success
that to-day, although not the first among
the great- Wagnerian artists, she has at
least a versatility that has rarely been
equaled by a German singer.
â¢ It may be said of Madame Gadski that
she is not too faithfully German. The art
that she brings to the music of Wagner
is not so much of a special school as it is
comprehensive, universal. She has com
mand not only of declamatory enuncia
tion, but possesses a beautiful legato style
and a well-defined mezza voce.
Her voice this year seems to be more
symmetrical and the Insecurity of her at
tack In the upper register, which was no
ticeable when. she sang here before, seems
to have disappeared. There is the same
wavering of the pitch that is characteris
tic of her singing, but she compensates
for this fault by abundant artistic quali
ties. Her intense dramaticism is delight
ful, for it springs from a genuine tem
perament and Is utterly different from the
artificial fervor of so many of the Ger
mans.
Mr. Bispham's singing is an example of
what the perfection of method can
achieve when taken Jn conjunction with
fundamental qualities of. more than or
dinary worth. In him the artist is para
mount. .One. finds In him more than
aught else the masterful grasp of the in
tellectual purpose of what he sings. His
voice is naturally full and- resonant, his
art makes it. rich and sonorous. His shad
ing and use of tone color is nothing short
of marvelous, his control Is wonderful and
his phrasing absolute perfection. He has
the mesa dl voce. a generous volume and
equable tone quality. He is unsensatlonal,
sincere; conscious of his powers no doubt
and conscious too perhaps of his defects,
which. If he have any, he cleverly con
ceals with his consummate art. And
that Is art.
Anton Schott sang excerpts from the
muplc of "Tannhauser" at times most ef
fectively. He did particularly well in the
Venus song and the narrative at the end
of the opera, but aside from the fact that
he is very much past his prime there is
evident in his singing of passages that
call for varied vocalization an effort at
color and expression that is rather more
studied than refined.
The Interest and success of the season
promise to grow with each succeeding
concert, " PORTER G ARXETT.

f l Conduct of a- Bival^Or- -
â¢'â¢ â¢' : Â¦':Â¦Â¦Â¦ < : â¢;â¢ ganization. . '* v- .
â¢ : The " management ' of -the Columbia- Ath
letic Club Is pursuing a. "policy which will

ism ih T thls 'city. Not satisfied 'with hav

bag. He haughtily announced that he did
not care a "rap" what the National Club
did, as the Columbia Club had offered
that amount and he would flght under its
auspices. Manager Gibbs was thunder
struck at this statement, and he went In
search of Manager Crowley and demanded
an explanation. A heated discussion fol
lowed, but nothing came of It.
Purtell has acted unwisely In the mat
ter. He came here under contract to flght
before the National Club, but without
even a "thank you" throws it over for
higher money. The Columbia Club Is even
greater . at fault. According to one fol
lower of the pastime, the woods are full
of fighters and that club could easily have
matched two good men without encroach
ing upon the preserves of the National
Club.

Purcell, which Is to be fought before tne
Sacramento Athletic Club on the 21st inst.

to come here and meet some 138-pound
man.

ACCIDENT TO A STRANGER.
George Braham Walks Into an Ele-
vator Shaft and Is Badly Injured.
George F. Graham, a recent arrival
from Seattle, fell down an elevator shaft
in front of the Young Men's Christian
Association building last night and was
severely injured. The unfortunate man
is troubled with defective eyesight and
walked in thtf shaft unconscious of his
danger. His groans attracted the atten
tion of a citizen, who at once informed
Police Officer Keyes. The latter had him
removed to the Receiving Hospital, where
an examination disclosed that he had sus
tained a severe injury to the left side, a
contusion of the scalp, lacerated wound of
the left cheek, besides having both wrists
sprained. Graham claims that there was
no guard around the elevator, but this
is denied by the manager of the building.
OBSERVED ANNIVERSARY
OF ITS ORGANIZATION
Coast Seamen's TJnion Braves the Ele
ments With a Parade, Followed .
by Speeches and a Banquet. ' .
The Coast Seamen's Union celebrated
the fifteenth anniversary of its organiza
tion last night with a parade and a ban
quet, the success of which totally eclipsed
all former efforts in that direction. Sanu
wiched-in between the two events there
was speechmaking in the hall of the
union) at Market and East streets, and a
large audience was addressed by forceful
speakers on the issues that affect the
well-being of the American sailor.
The procession moved' from the head
quarters at 8 o'clock with Captain Ed
ward Anderson as grand marshal, assist
ed by Chris Jortall and ' Edward Rosen
berg aB chief aids. Three companies,
numbering 400 sailors. In- uniform, fol
lowed with two ..bands playing martial
airsâ Company A, . Captain Frank- John
son; Company B, Captain Thomas Fan
nerty, and Company C, Captain 'C.
Thompson. An advance guard of twenty
four men under Captain Helmgren headed
the parade, which marched down East
street to Folsom, .to Steuart, up. Market,
to Fourth, countermarching to Kearny, to
California, to Market and to the head
quarters.
The speeches began in the main hall as
soon as the vast audience * had . become
seated. Captain Anderson after stating
that March 6 was the "sailors" day of In
dependence." introduced Ed Rosenberg,
manager of;the Coast Seamen's Journal,
as the chairman of the- evening, and he
introduced Hon. James G.Maguire. After
the tumultuous applause which | greeted
Judge Maguire's appearance had subsided
he proceeded to recount the events leading
up to the Congressional fight made for
the seamen by which â¢ they were emanci
pated from' their thralldom. He traced
the struggle in the Fifty-third,- Fifty
fourth and Fifty-fifth Congresses, and the
final victory of the measure. Remarks
were also made by Nick Jortall and Chri3
Jortall, after which the: members- and
their guests adjourned to the banquet hall,
where an abundant repast was' served. Â¦

DISTRICT DELEGATES
Republican Committee of the Fourth
Congressional District to Hold a
Meeting at Pythian Castle
To-Night.
Yesterday Joseph Spear Jr., chairman of
the Republican Congressional Committee
of. the Fourth District, appointed a com
mittee, consisting -of Dr. P. A. Kearney,
P. H. Prendergast and A. Barnard to ar
range for a meeting of the entire commit
tee.
The members of the Fourth District
Committee are: Joseph Spear Jr., P. H.
Prendergast, A. Barnard, Dr. P. A. Kear
ney. J. l^evl Jr., A. Ruef, William Howe,
James McCabe, Albert Houston. R. H.
Williams, Charles Metzner, J. A. Hoey,

tig, Martin Brady, J. H. JCelson and
James Snook.
The subcommittee appointed yesterday
arranged for a meeting of the entire com
mittee in Crystal Hall, fvthian Castle,
SMS Market street, this evening. It is un
derstood that preliminary steps will be
taken for calling a district convention for
the purpose of choosing two delegates to
the Republican National Convention.
BOXERS in all their varieties took
part in the Olympic Athletic Club
tournament last night, and showed
the sport in all Its phases. There
were no knockouts, but in four of
the bouts the seconds threw up
the sponge, thus- saving their men from
needless punishment. The righting in the
majority of the ten bouts was of the hur
ricane order, the boxers standing up
gamely under the most trying conditions.
John L. Scholes of Toronto, Canada,
champion 125-pound amateur boxer of
America, England and Canada, was the
first of the Eastern champions to appear.
He created a most favorable impression
by his manly bearing in the ring, and was
given an ovation when he appeared first,
and another and. if anything, a more cor
dial one. after he displayed his remark
able prowess as a boxer when he defeated
F. Macdonald of the Reliance Athletic
Club. Macdonald is a stocky boxer of the
Sharkey type. He rushed at Scholes,
head down and swinging wildly, but was
unable to make any stand against the
clever Easterner. The latter is tall and
rather slightly built, being reminiscent of
Kid McCoy. He is a master In the art
of jabbing. An elastic bandage over the
elbow Joint of his left arm heightens the
Impression that he is not so strong as he
m He landed at will on Macdonald In the
first round. The Reliance Club man was
swinging all the time trying to place an
effective blow, but to no avail. Twice
Scboles slipped on the floor of the elevated
ring, and the admirers of Macdonald
thought their man was doing some effec
tive work. This idea was dispelled in the
ANOTHER HOLE
PUNCHED IN THE
NEW CHARTER
Stenographers Are Governed
by State Laws Which
Fix Their Fees.
A hole of considerable size was knocked
In the' charter yesterday by a decision
handed" down by Superior Judge Seawell
in the suit- instituted by Luke Kavanagh.
stenographer of Department 6, to compel
Treasurer Brooks to pay him the fees pro
vided for by the State laws. The court
holds that criminal cases are exclusively
a matter of State concern and that tha
city and county representatives exceeded
their authority when they incorporated in
the charter a provision directly affecting:
the preservation of records of the trials
of criminals, in which the city and county
has no interest other than it has In com
mon with all the people of the State in
upholding: the administration of the laws.
In passing on the issues raised In the
case Judge Seawell, In part, said:
The question presented, for determination is
whether in thia city and county section 274 of
the Code of Civil Procedure. Â«o far as it pro-,
vides for the compensation of reporters in crim
inal cases, has been superseded by the section
of \he charter fixing' stenographer's fees.
While it may be conceded that a provision In
a charter not relating to municipal affairs may
be valid in the absence of a general law upon
the subject, if there is such a general law, the
charter must yield to the general law.
Criminal cases are exclusively a matter of
State concern. They are prosecuted in the
name and by the authority of the people of the
State. A particular county or city and county
has no Interest In them other than it has In
common with all the people of the State In up
holding the administration of the laws. .
It may be observed that the section of the
charter under consideration' does not fix the
salary of reporters in the criminal departments
of the Superior Court at any definite sum, but
merely declares that th#y shall each receive an
annual salary not exceeding $2400. If any board
or officer has the power under the charter to
determine the amount within this maximum
limit it does not appear to have been exer
cised.
Again, the section of the charter, under con
sideration provides for the payment of an an
nual salary to the "stwographers In the crim
inal departments of the Superior Court." Some
of the departments of that court are engaged
In the trial of both criminal and civil cases.
Only a small part of his time may- be occupied
in the trial of criminal cases, and yet the re
porter In such' a department Â¦ would receive his
annual salary and also fees in civil cases from
private litigants, while the reporter In a de
partment occupied with criminal cases exclu
sively would receive only the annual salary
For the reasons above stated, the demurrer to
the petition will -be overruled. Â¦
The overruling of 'defendant's demurrer
leaves him without further defense, and
the provision in the charter regulating
stenographers' fees is now a nullity

AUTHORITIES DECLARE
â¢ THE PLAGUE
Cannot Obtain a Footing: Here
Surgeon General W. X. Van
Reypen of the navy is an au
thority on the bubonic
plague. In discussing the
probability of its reaching
San Francisco he says: "The
climatic conditions of the
United States preclude the
possibility of the plague ever
getting within this country.
It is a- disease peculiar to the
Orient, and seldom, if ever,
attacks Europeans. The bu
bonic plague with reference
to the United States is like
beri beri. A Japanese vessel
once brought a number - of
cases of this disease to New
York. Those afflicted were
isolated and soon recovered.
There is absolutely no danger
of 'the plague ever getting
here."
The acting, surgeon general
of the army,- Colonel Charles
H. Alden, says: "The quar
antine service is so efficient
that the bubonic plague could
never obtain a foothold in the
United States, even though
the conditions were favorable.
I don't know of a single case
of plague in the United States.
It is a disease which flourishes
only where there is dirt and
filth."

TRIED TWICE TO END
HER MISERABLE LIFE
Sirs. Erickson Vainly Attempts to
Commit Suicide in the City
Prison.
While temporarily Insane from the tf-
f>cts of liquor, Mrs. Emma Erlckson, a
middle-aged woman, last night made two
attempts to' commit suicide in the City
Prison. Yesterday afternoon the woman
was arrested on a charge of drunkenness
and placed In the cell set apart for in
ebriated females. Several hours later
ehe securely lied Â«. ribbon around her
threat and vainly endeavored to strangle
herf^lf. The screams of another inmate
of the cell attracted the attention of a
trilst y. who hastily summoned Turnkey
I Â»onohue and Desk Sergeant Lindheimer.
Thpy quickly opened the cell door and re
moved the ribbon from Mrs. Erickson's
throat.
â¢ Shortly afterward she secured another
pl*-cf of ribbon and again tried to strangle
herself. When discovered her tongue was
Â¦protruding and she otherwise seemed in
thf* throes of death. She was hastily re
nioved to the Receiving Hospital in the
patrol wagon, where, under the treatment
of the surgeon on duty, she soon recov
ered. When questioned she answered in
coherently, and as it was evident that her
mind was tottering it was decided to
charge her with insanity. She was then
removed to the detention ward, pending
an examination by the Commissioners of
Lunacy as to her mental condition.

THIEVES SNEAK
AWAY WITH THE
CONSUL'S JEWELS
Bolivia's Representative in
Jail on a Charge of
Drunkenness.
When He Awoke in a Prison Cell His
Diamonds, Gold Watch, Money
and Overcoat Were
Gone.
Several detectives are searching the city
for the men or women who robbed Bele
sario Mello, the Consul for Bolivia, some
time on Sunday night or early Monday
morning, he does not know which or
Â¦where.
Mello went to a downtown restaurant
on Sunday night with some congenial
friends for dinner. He drank copiously of
the wine that is red and when he recov
ered his senses on Monday morning he
was In a cell in the City Prison with a
number of men with whom he had not
been accustomed to associate. He could
net realize what had happened him, and
when he was taken into Judge Cabanlss
court with other "drunks" he meekly told
thÂ« Judge it was his first offense and was
discharged.
When he started out Sunday night he
had a diamond pin. a diamond ring, a
very valuable gold watch and chain and a
well filled purse: but all these had dis
appeared, besides his overcoat and gloves.
He wondered why his hat had not been
appropriated also. He met a friend, who
advised him to report his loss to the
police, and they went to Captain Bohen
and gave a description of the stolen arti
cles. He said he had not the remotest
Idea of what happened him after he was
at dinner in the restaurant, but he ex
pressed a strong desire to get his prop
erty back and no questions asked.
About 1 o'clock Monday morning Police
man Geimann's attention was attracted
by some one shouting and singing on
Geary street, near Grant avenue. He
went In the direction of the sound and
found a man clinging to a lamp-post.
Geiniann asked him his name, but the
only answer he rot was, "I'm the Consul,
I'm the Consul.' Geimann called to Po
liceman Collins, who happened along, and
as they could not get any sense out of the
man he was taken to the patrol box and
cent to the City Prison in the patrol
wagon on a charge of "drunk." There
he was able to give something like his
r.ame. When searched nothing was found
In his pockets.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, ; MARCH 7, 1900.
POLICE KEEPING
QUARANTINE GUARD
OVER CHINATOWN
Suspicious Death of a La
borer Beings Investigated
by Dr. O'Brien.
Some Doubt as to the Nature of the
Case, but the Locality Has
Been Isolated ' for
Safety's Sake.
Â«Â¦Â¦Â¦''.:'Â¦' .
What is thought to be the bubonic
plague, according to Health Officer
O'Brien, has been discovered in China
town. One Chinaman living at 10M Du
pont street succumbed to the disease late
last night. Immediately after his death
was reported to Chief of Police Sullivan
the Chief detailed all of the available
officers to exclude the whites from China
town. Strict orders were also given to
police officers to carefully guard the va
rious, streets leading to the Chinese
quarter to prevent all Chinese from leav
ing.
By these means the head of, the Police
Department, as well as the health author
ities, hope to prevent any spread of the
disease. .
That the plague existed in the Chinese
quarter was kept a secret until late last
night, when the Chinese laborer died sud
denly under suspicious circumstances. -
A police officer, suspecting that he was
a victim of the disease, at once notified
Health Officer O'Brien. Dr. O'Brien ex
amined the body and came to the con
clusion that the officer's suspicion's were
correct. He ordered a strict quarantine
of the house, after which he promptly
telephoned to Chief Sullivan.
. The Chief was attending a meeting of
the Police Commissioners when he re
ceived the message. Without waiting to
listen to the concluding words of an ad
dress which was being delivered by an at
torney, who with eloquence was vigor
ously endeavoring to save the star of an
offending policeman, Sullivan repaired to
his private office. He then sent for Cap
tain Wittman, who had been apprised of
the suspicious death of the Chinaman,
and hurriedly told him of the message he
had received from Dr. O'Brien.
The Chief was at first in favor of with
drawing his two squads from the Chinese
district, but as he feared that the rival
highbinders would take advantage of the
situation and renew hostilities, he de
cided to keep them there. He, however,
promptly made out a detail of all his
available men to report at once to their
various captains for duty In Chinatown.
They will be assigned to positions along
the narrow streets leading into Chinatown
with strict instructions not to allow any
body to enter or leave. ,' ' ,_ /
The health authorities are satisfied that
they will succeed in wiping out the plague
before it can claim another victim. A
strict quarantine Â¦ will be established to
day and every, precaution will be taken to
prevent a spread of the disease.
The lodging-house where the unfortun
ate Chinese succumbed to the effects of
the so-called plague will be thoroughly
fumigated this morning and the occu
pants will not be' allowed outside.
Realizing the necessity for prompt ac
tion In preventing the disease from
spreading Chief Sullivan intends, if the
gravity of the situation requires It, to de
tail every member of the department to
guard the entrances to Chinatown and
prevent the Mongolians from leaving.

side, where he could see his next oppo
nent, \V. J. Leonard, in action.
The fighting blood of Leonard was
aroused in his bout with Joe Sullivan and
he heedlessly committed a breach of ring
etiquette, for which he was roundly hissed.
He had Jabbed Sullivan until the latter
showed unmistakable signs of having ex
tracted all the sport there was in the
game for him. Early in the third. round
Sullivan's seconds threw up the -sponge
and Leonard's seconds Jumped into the
ring. But before they could stop him he
rushed at Sullivan, who was badly dis
tressed, and knocked him to the floor. He
apologized for his error, but the specta
tors hissed him from the ring. Leonard
knocked Sullivan down repeatedly during
the bout and the latter usually stayed
down as long as- possible. From appear
ances the star bout of the tournament will
be between Leonard and Scholes. in the
final of the special class, which Includes
the boxers weighing 125 pounds. Leonard
is much the heavier muscled, but it re
mains to be seen if Scholes 1 greater
familiarity with ring tactics will not
counterbalance this.
Only one bout was fought in the heavy
weight class, J. A. Munroe, the giant foot
ball player, meeting and winning from
Charles Mil ward, who weighed some forty
pounds less than he did. When "Bob"
McGibben. the announcer, said he was a
heavy-weight, a ripple of laughter swept
through the gymnasium, as any one with
half an eye could see he was. The.dis
parity In the size of the men was such
as to Indicate that Milward had no
chance. Munroe hustled him about the
ring and once in his impetuosity he near
ly dashed headlong through the ropes be
fore he could check himself. Milward
was down for six seconds in the first
round and had difficulty in lasting it out.
A left swing flush on the mouth nearly
put him out of business. In the second
round he sustained a badly cut mouth and
went down hard, taking eight seconds on
the floor. There were murmurs among
the spectators that the unequal battle be
stopped. It -proceeded some time into the
